Commentary
This passage discusses existential conditional propositions (muntasheera and wujoodiyya). The general existential (muntasheera 'amma) asserts that the conditional holds at some time (lit. 'in a time of the times'). Its contradictory is a general possibility (mumkina 'amma) that differs in quantity and quality: 'Not: whenever A is B, it is C at all' by general possibility. The special existential (here called 'mashruta khassa' in the text but likely refers to the special existential) is similar but not perpetual; it only holds at some times. Its contradictory is either the same general possibility or a perpetual proposition that agrees in quality. Finally, the author mentions the non-necessary existential (wujoodiyya la daruriyya), which is an existential conditional that is not necessary. Its contradictory is said to be either a perpetual proposition differing in quantity and quality, or necessity (al-darura). The text breaks off here, but the pattern is consistent: the contradictory of a non-necessary existential is either a perpetual (i.e., 'always') or a necessary proposition, depending on the type.

Contradictories of Conditional Propositions (Muttasila)
Author lists the contradictories for various types of conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory for each type of conditional proposition (absolute perpetual, general conditional, particular conditional) based on the logical relation (differing in quantity and quality or agreeing in quality).

Core Idea
The contradictory of a conditional depends on its modal time: perpetuity or generality, necessity or possibility.

In this passage, the author systematically lists the contradictory pairs for various types of conditional propositions. Understanding these contradictory relations is crucial for constructing valid arguments and refuting false claims in Islamic logic. We will walk through the three main types: absolute perpetual, general conditional, and particular conditional, and see how each is contradicted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of conditionals
The passage distinguishes three types: absolute perpetual (دائمة مطلقة), general conditional (عرفية عامة), and particular conditional (عرفية خاصة). Note the condition each carries.
Absolute perpetual holds at all times; general conditional holds as long as the subject remains; particular conditional holds under a specific condition.
2
Find the contradictory for the absolute perpetual
The author states that the contradictory of 'دائمة مطلقة' is 'مطلقة عامة' (absolute general) differing in quantity and quality. For example, 'Always when A is B, it is C' is contradicted by 'Not always when A is B, it is C' with absolute generality.
3
Find the contradictory for the general conditional
For 'عرفية عامة', the contradictory is 'مطلقة عامة حينية' (absolute general under-a-condition) differing in quantity and quality. Example: 'Whenever A is B, it is C as long as it is B' is contradicted by 'Not whenever A is B, it is C when it is B'.
4
Find the contradictory for the particular conditional
For 'عرفية خاصة', two possibilities: either 'مطلقة عامة حينية' differing in quantity and quality, or 'دائمة' (perpetual) agreeing in quality. Example: 'Whenever A is B, it is C not always but as long as B' is contradicted by 'Not whenever A is B, it is C when it is B' or 'If A is B, it is C always'.
The first option is the standard contradictory; the second is an alternative when the conditional is not perpetual.
5
Generalize to other modal propositions
The author notes that the same pattern applies to other modal propositions (الضروريات) with only two variances: absolute possibility and absolute generality. This completes the framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموافقة كيفًا كما يقال كلما كان «أب» كان «ج» لا بالضرورة، ليس كلَّما كان «أب» كان «ج» دائما ، أو قد يكون إذا كان أب كان «ج» بالضرورة؛ هذا هو الكلام في الضرورة واللا ضرورة. وأما في الدوام واللا دوام فنقول: وما يدل على النقيض في الدائمة المطلقة فذلك هو المطلقة العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال دائمًا إذا كان أب كان «ج»، ليس دائما إذا كان «أب» كان «ج» بالإطلاق العام. وفي العرفية العامة، المطلقة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال كلَّما كان «أب» كان «ج» ما دام «ب»، ليس كلما كان «أب» كان «ج» حين هو «ب» بالإطلاق العام. وفي العرفية الخاصة هو المطلقة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال كلَّما كان «أب» كان «ج» لا دائما بل ما دام «ب»، ليس كلَّما كان «أب» كان «ج» حين هو «ب» بالإطلاق العام أو قد يكون إذا كان «أب» كان «ج» دائما. وعلى هذا في الغير من القضايا الباقية التي مرَّ ذكرها في الضروريات فإنه لا تفاوت بينهما وبين الدائمات في التناقض إلا في جهتي الإمكان العام والإطلاق العام على ما عرفت فهذه كلها من جملة ما يكون من اللوازم معرفته في المتصلات.
Agreement in quality: as is said, 'Whenever A is B, it is C' not necessarily, 'Not whenever A is B, it is C' always, or it may be that if A is B, it is C necessarily; this is the discussion on necessity and non-necessity. As for perpetuity and non-perpetuity, we say: what indicates the contradictory in the absolute perpetual (conditional) is the absolute general (conditional) differing in quantity and quality, as is said: 'Always when A is B, it is C', 'Not always when A is B, it is C' with absolute generality. In the general conditional, it is the absolute general under-a-condition differing in quantity and quality, as is said: 'Whenever A is B, it is C so long as it is B', 'Not whenever A is B, it is C when it is B' with absolute generality. In the particular conditional, it is the absolute general under-a-condition differing in quantity and quality, or the perpetual agreeing in quality, as is said: 'Whenever A is B, it is C not always but so long as B', 'Not whenever A is B, it is C when it is B' with absolute generality, or it may be that when A is B, it is C always. And likewise for the rest of the remaining propositions that were mentioned in the necessaries, for there is no difference between them and the perpetuals in contradiction except regarding the two modes of absolute possibility and absolute generality, as you have learned. All these are among the things whose knowledge is necessary in conditionals.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory pairs for different types of conditional propositions (muttasilat) under the modes of necessity and perpetuity. The author first discusses necessity, then moves to perpetuity. For each type—absolute perpetual, general conditional, and particular conditional—he states which type of proposition serves as its contradictory, noting the logical relation (differing in quantity and quality, or agreeing in quality). Islamic examples: Consider the conditional 'If this liquid is wine, it is forbidden' as a perpetual conditional; its contradictory is 'It is not the case that always when this liquid is wine, it is forbidden' (absolute general). This meticulous classification is essential for understanding valid contradiction in complex modal statements.

Key Takeaway

Contradictories of Necessary Disjunctive Propositions
Author explains contradictories for necessary disjunctives under absolute, conditioned, and temporal modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory for each type of necessary disjunctive proposition (absolute, conditioned, temporal) and explain how it differs in quantity and quality.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a necessary disjunctive is a possible proposition differing in quantity and quality.

In logic, every proposition has a contradictory. For disjunctive propositions (munfasilat), the rule is similar to conditionals: the contradictory must be a proposition of general possibility that negates the original while altering its quantity and quality. This lesson unpacks that rule for the various sub-types under the mode of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal force
Determine if the original disjunctive is qualified by 'necessarily' (بالضرورة). The contradictory will be a 'possible general' (الممكنة العامة) proposition.
2
Check quantity and quality
The contradictory always differs in both quantity (from universal to not-universal) and quality (from affirmative to negative). For example, 'Necessarily every A is either B or C' (universal affirmative) has the contradictory 'Not every A is either B or C' (particular negative) with general possibility.
3
Handle conditioned and temporal variants
For conditioned (مشروطة) and temporal (وقتية) disjunctives, the contradictory retains the same temporal/conditional qualifier but with possibility. For general conditioned, the contradictory is 'Not every A is either B or C when it is A' with general possibility.
4
Recognize the alternative for particular cases
For 'not always' particular conditioned/temporal disjunctives, there is an additional contradictory: a perpetual (دائمة) proposition that agrees in quality but differs in quantity. For example, 'Some A is always either B or C' (perpetual particular affirmative) is also a contradictory.
This is the 'الدائمة الموافقة كيفا' option.
5
Verify with the template
Use the patterns provided in the text for each sub-type to confirm you have correctly identified the contradictory.
The author follows a systematic pattern: original type → contradictory type + examples.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في المنفصلات فالكلام فيها بحسب الجهات نحو الكلام في المتّصلات. فما يدل على النقيض في الضرورية المطلقة هو الممكنة العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» بالإمكان العام. وفي المشروطة العامة هو الممكنة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما ب وإما «ج» ما دام «أ»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» حين هو «أ» بالإمكان العام. وفي المشروطة الخاصة هو الممكنة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» لا دائما بل ما دام «أ»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» حين هو «أ» بالإمكان العام، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما «ج» دائما . وأما في الوقتية العامة فهو الممكنة العامة الوقتية المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» وقت كذا، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» وقت كذا بالإمكان العام. وفي الوقتية الخاصة هو الممكنة العامة الوقتية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» وقت كذا بالإمكان العام، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما «ج» دائما. وعلى هذا في الغير من القضايا الموجهة بجهة الضرورة. وأما في الموجهة بجهة الدوام ففي الدائمة المطلقة هو المطلقة العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال دائما كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» بالإطلاق العام. وفي العرفية العامة هو المطلقة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» ما دام «أ»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» حين هو «أ» بالإطلاق العام. وفي العرفية الخاصة هو المطلقة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» حين هو «أ» بالإطلاق العام، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما «ج» دائما . وعلى هذا في الغير من القضايا الباقية فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
As for disjunctives, the discussion regarding them according to the modes is similar to the discussion on conditionals. So what indicates the contradictory in the necessary absolute is the possible general differing in quantity and quality, as is said: 'Necessarily every A is either B or C', 'Not every A is either B or C' with general possibility. In the general conditioned, it is the possible general under-a-condition differing in quantity and quality, as is said: 'Necessarily every A is either B or C as long as it is A', 'Not every A is either B or C when it is A' with general possibility. In the particular conditioned, it is the possible general under-a-condition differing in quantity and quality, or the perpetual agreeing in quality, as is said: 'Necessarily every A is either B or C not always but as long as A', 'Not every A is either B or C when it is A' with general possibility, or some of it is either B or C always. As for the general temporal, it is the possible general temporal differing in quantity and quality, as is said: 'Necessarily every A is either B or C at time T', 'Not every A is either B or C at time T' with general possibility. In the particular temporal, it is the possible general temporal differing in quantity and quality, or the perpetual agreeing in quality, as is said: 'Necessarily every A is either B or C not always but at time T', 'Not every A is either B or C at time T' with general possibility, or some of it is either B or C always. And likewise for the rest of the propositions qualified with the mode of necessity. As for those qualified with the mode of perpetuity, in the absolute perpetual it is the absolute general differing in quantity and quality, as is said: 'Always every A is either B or C', 'Not every A is either B or C' with absolute generality. In the general conditional, it is the absolute general under-a-condition differing in quantity and quality, as is said: 'Every A is either B or C as long as A', 'Not every A is either B or C when it is A' with absolute generality. In the particular conditional, it is the absolute general under-a-condition differing in quantity and quality, or the perpetual agreeing in quality, as is said: 'Every A is either B or C when it is A' with absolute generality, or some of it is either B or C always. And likewise for the rest of the remaining propositions, so consider as you have learned, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage systematically defines the contradictories for disjunctive propositions (munfasilat) under the two main modes: necessity and perpetuity. For each sub-type—absolute necessary, general conditioned, particular conditioned, general temporal, particular temporal (under necessity), and absolute perpetual, general conditional, particular conditional (under perpetuity)—the author identifies the contradictory proposition, which differs in quantity and quality (or sometimes agrees in quality but differs in quantity). The pattern mirrors that of conditionals. Islamic example: The disjunctive 'Necessarily every legal act is either obligatory or permissible' has its contradictory 'It is possible that not every legal act is either obligatory or permissible' (possible general). This careful specification is crucial for ensuring that contradictory pairs are correctly identified in logical reasoning.

Checkpoint
For the necessary absolute disjunctive 'Necessarily every valid marriage contract is either permanent or temporary', what is its contradictory?
A) 'It is possible that every valid marriage contract is neither permanent nor temporary.'
B) 'Not every valid marriage contract is either permanent or temporary' with general possibility.
C) 'Always every valid marriage contract is either permanent or temporary.'
D) 'It is necessary that some valid marriage contract is not either permanent or temporary.'

Key Takeaway
For necessary disjunctives, the contradictory is always a possible general proposition that differs in both quantity and quality, except for particular conditioned/temporal cases where a perpetual agreeing contradictory is also valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Contradictories of Perpetual Disjunctive Propositions
Author explains contradictories for perpetual disjunctives under absolute, general conditional, and particular conditional modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the same pattern to disjunctives qualified with perpetuity (دائم) and identify their contradictories using absolute general or absolute general under-a-condition.

Core Idea
For perpetual disjunctives, the contradictory is an absolute general proposition, not a possible one.

The mode of perpetuity (دوام) is the second major modal class for disjunctives. The pattern mirrors necessity, but instead of 'possible general', the contradictory uses 'absolute general' (مطلقة عامة) for absolute perpetual and 'absolute general under-a-condition' for conditioned types. This lesson covers the absolute perpetual, general conditional, and particular conditional sub-types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the perpetual mode
Determine if the original disjunctive is qualified by 'always' (دائما) or 'as long as' (ما دام) etc. The contradictory will be an 'absolute general' (مطلقة عامة) proposition.
2
Check quantity and quality
As in necessity, the contradictory differs in both quantity and quality. For absolute perpetual: 'Always every A is either B or C' (universal affirmative) has contradictory 'Not every A is either B or C' with absolute generality (particular negative).
3
Handle general conditional
For general conditional (عرفية عامة): 'Every A is either B or C as long as A' has contradictory 'Not every A is either B or C when it is A' with absolute generality.
4
Recognize the alternative for particular conditional
For particular conditional (عرفية خاصة), the contradictory is either the absolute general under-a-condition differing in quantity and quality, or a perpetual proposition agreeing in quality (الدائمة الموافقة كيفا). For example, 'Every A is either B or C when it is A' (with absolute generality? Actually careful: the original is 'كل أ إما ب وإما ج حين هو أ' without دائما, but the contradictory options are 'ليس كل أ إما ب وإما ج حين هو أ بالإطلاق العام' or 'بعضه إما ب وإما ج دائما'.
The perpetual agreeing option is a particular affirmative perpetual: 'Some A is always either B or C'.
5
Apply to other types
The same method extends to the remaining propositions qualified with perpetuity, following the pattern described.

Defining Simple Conversion ('Aks Mustawi)
Author defines simple conversion and gives his own refined definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple conversion and list its three conditions: preservation of quality, truth, and exchange of the whole subject and predicate.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving quality and truth.

In this passage, the author distinguishes simple conversion from other immediate inferences. He first presents the standard definition from the majority of philosophers, then offers his own refined version that specifies exchanging the 'whole thing judged about' and the 'whole thing judged by'. Understanding this definition is essential for correctly applying conversion to categorical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the definition given by the majority of philosophers and the author's own definition. Note the difference: the majority says 'making the subject a predicate and the predicate a subject,' while the author says 'replacing the whole subject with the whole predicate.'
2
Compare the definitions
The author's definition emphasizes that it is the entire subject and predicate that are exchanged, not just the terms themselves. This is more precise.
3
Note the restriction to categoricals
The author explicitly says this definition is for categorical propositions (ḥamliyyāt); simple conversion in conditionals is different.
4
List the preservation conditions
Both definitions agree that the quality (affirmation/negation) and the truth value (truth/falsehood) must remain unchanged after conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني من المباحث فإنه في العكس المستوي / أما العكس فإنه من لوازم القضية كما مرَّ من قبل، غير أنه يسمى بهذا الاسم من بينها لما أنه بحسب تبدل الأجزاء أو بحسب ما يقابل كل واحد منها. والأول منهما هو العكس المستوي، والجمهور من الحكماء عرفوه بأنه عبارة عن تصيير الموضوع محمولا، والمحمول موضوعًا مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله؛ والصدق والكذب بحاله أيضًا : غير أنه هو التعريف المخصوص بالحمليات؛ إذ العكس المستوي في الشرطيات كان بمعزل عنه. والحق فيه أن يقال إنه عبارة عن تبديل المحكوم عليه بتمامه محكوما به ، والمحكوم به محكوما عليه مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله ، والصدق والكذب بحاله كذلك في سائر المواد. ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف هو وقد يكون من القضايا الكاذبة ما يكون عكسه صادقًا فيقال مثلا كل موجود ممكن، وإنه مع كذبه ينعكس عكسا صادقًا وهو قولنا بعض الممكن موجود لكنا نقول إنه وإن كان كاذبًا فقد كان مشتملا على ما يكون صادقًا في نفسه وهو قولنا بعض الموجود ممكن، فذلك العكس من حيث هو صادق عكس هذا الصادق لا عكس ذلك الكاذب حتى إذا يكن مشتملا على ذلك أو يكون مشتملا عليه لكنا لا نعلمه؛ فلا يمكننا أن نقول إنه ينعكس عكسًا صادقًا. وإن قيل لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان العكس في مثل قولنا كل إنسان حيوان عكس ما يكون في ضمنه وهو قولنا بعض الإنسان حيوان لا عكس ذلك. فنقول هب أنه كذلك لكن لم قلتم بأنه لا يمكن بل يمكن أن يكون اعتبار الصدق في العكس باعتبار الصدق في الأصل مطلقًا سواء كان بحسب الصدق في كل فرد أو بحسب الصدق في البعض منها حتى إذا كان مشتملا على الصدق بوجه ما فإنه ينعكس عكسا صادقا والاتحاد في العكس كلية كانت القضية أو جزئية يدل على هذا دلالة ظاهرة تعرف بالتأمل. وأيضًا قولهم مع بقاء الصدق والكذب بحاله يدل على انحصار الأصل في كونه صادقًا أو كاذبا ؛ والانحصار لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون المراد من كونه
As for the second investigation, it is concerning simple conversion. As for conversion, it is one of the necessary concomitants of the proposition, as has passed previously, except that it is called by this name among them because it is according to the change of the parts or according to what corresponds to each one of them. The first of them is simple conversion, and the majority of philosophers have defined it as making the subject a predicate and the predicate a subject while preserving the negation and affirmation as they are; and truth and falsehood as they are also, except that this is a definition particular to categoricals, since simple conversion in conditionals is remote from it. The truth in it is that one should say it is the replacement of the whole thing judged about (al-mahkum 'alayh) with the thing judged by (al-mahkum bih), and the thing judged by with the thing judged about, while preserving negation and affirmation as they are, and truth and falsehood as they are likewise in all materials. Then a speaker may say: How is that, when there are false propositions whose converse is true? For example, it is said: Everything existent is possible, and despite its falsehood, it converts to a true converse, which is our saying: Some possible is existent. But we say: Although it is false, it includes what is true in itself, which is our saying: Some existent is possible. So that converse, inasmuch as it is true, is the converse of this true [proposition], not the converse of that false one, unless it includes that [truth] or we do not know it; so we cannot say that it converts to a true converse. And if it is said: If it were as you mentioned, then the converse in something like our saying: Every human is an animal would be the converse of what is within it, which is our saying: Some human is an animal, not the converse of that [universal]. So we say: Granted that it is so, but why did you say that it is not possible? Rather, it is possible that the consideration of truth in the converse is according to the truth in the original absolutely, whether according to truth in every individual or according to truth in some of them. So if it includes truth in some way, then it converts to a true converse. And the identity in conversion, whether the proposition is universal or particular, indicates this with a clear indication known by reflection. And also, their saying 'with preserving truth and falsehood as they are' indicates the restriction of the original to being true or false; and restriction is not possible except that the intended meaning of it...
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion ('aks mustawi) as an immediate inference where the subject and predicate are swapped while preserving the quality (affirmation/negation) and truth value. The author first gives the standard definition of the majority: making the subject a predicate and vice versa, but notes this is specific to categoricals; for conditionals, conversion is different. Then he provides his own definition: the whole thing judged about (the subject) becomes the thing judged by (the predicate), and the whole thing judged by becomes the thing judged about, preserving negation/affirmation and truth/falsehood in all materials (i.e., regardless of content). An objection is then raised: some false propositions have true converses, e.g., 'Everything existent is possible' is false, but its converse 'Some possible is existent' is true. The author replies that the false original includes a true proposition within it ('Some existent is possible'), and the true converse is actually the converse of that underlying true proposition, not the false one. Another objection: if we take 'Every human is an animal', its converse should be the converse of 'Some human is an animal' (which is true) rather than the original universal. The author responds that this is acceptable; truth in the original can be considered absolutely, whether per se or per accidens. The definition's requirement that truth and falsehood remain the same means the original must be either true or false; the converse will have the same truth status as the original (or as the underlying truth).

Key Terms
عَكْس مُسْتَوِي
المَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ
المَحْكُوم بِهِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is preserved in simple conversion?
A) Subject and predicate only
B) Quality and truth
C) Quantity and quality
D) Only truth

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion is a valid immediate inference that exchanges subject and predicate as wholes while preserving quality and truth, and it applies only to categorical propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is haram.' Perform simple conversion: what is the converse? Is it true? Check the conditions.
?
Find a universal affirmative proposition from a fiqh text (e.g., 'Every marriage requires consent'). Write its simple converse and verify if truth is preserved.

Responding to the Objection: Can a False Proposition Have a True Converse?
Author responds to objections that a false proposition can have a true converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's response to the objection that a false proposition can have a true converse, and distinguish between the converse of a false proposition and the converse of an underlying true proposition.

Core Idea
A false proposition's true converse is actually the converse of an implied true proposition.

After defining simple conversion, the author anticipates an objection: consider the false proposition 'Every existent is possible.' Its converse 'Some possible is existent' is true. How can conversion preserve truth if a false original has a true converse? The author's answer clarifies that the false proposition contains a true proposition, and the true converse belongs to that underlying truth.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
An objector says: the false proposition 'Every existent is possible' has a true converse 'Some possible is existent'. This seems to violate the condition that conversion preserves truth.
2
Identify the author's first response
The author replies that the false original includes a true proposition ('Some existent is possible'), and the true converse is actually the converse of that true proposition, not the false one.
3
Consider the second objection
Another objection: for 'Every human is an animal', its converse 'Some animal is a human' could be seen as the converse of the implied 'Some human is an animal' rather than the original universal.
4
Understand the author's defense
The author argues that truth in the original can be considered absolutely, whether per se or per accidens. If the original includes truth in some way (e.g., by containing a true particular), the converse is considered with respect to that truth.

وأما الثاني من المباحث فإنه في العكس المستوي / أما العكس فإنه من لوازم القضية كما مرَّ من قبل، غير أنه يسمى بهذا الاسم من بينها لما أنه بحسب تبدل الأجزاء أو بحسب ما يقابل كل واحد منها. والأول منهما هو العكس المستوي، والجمهور من الحكماء عرفوه بأنه عبارة عن تصيير الموضوع محمولا، والمحمول موضوعًا مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله؛ والصدق والكذب بحاله أيضًا : غير أنه هو التعريف المخصوص بالحمليات؛ إذ العكس المستوي في الشرطيات كان بمعزل عنه. والحق فيه أن يقال إنه عبارة عن تبديل المحكوم عليه بتمامه محكوما به ، والمحكوم به محكوما عليه مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله ، والصدق والكذب بحاله كذلك في سائر المواد. ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف هو وقد يكون من القضايا الكاذبة ما يكون عكسه صادقًا فيقال مثلا كل موجود ممكن، وإنه مع كذبه ينعكس عكسا صادقًا وهو قولنا بعض الممكن موجود لكنا نقول إنه وإن كان كاذبًا فقد كان مشتملا على ما يكون صادقًا في نفسه وهو قولنا بعض الموجود ممكن، فذلك العكس من حيث هو صادق عكس هذا الصادق لا عكس ذلك الكاذب حتى إذا يكن مشتملا على ذلك أو يكون مشتملا عليه لكنا لا نعلمه؛ فلا يمكننا أن نقول إنه ينعكس عكسًا صادقًا. وإن قيل لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان العكس في مثل قولنا كل إنسان حيوان عكس ما يكون في ضمنه وهو قولنا بعض الإنسان حيوان لا عكس ذلك. فنقول هب أنه كذلك لكن لم قلتم بأنه لا يمكن بل يمكن أن يكون اعتبار الصدق في العكس باعتبار الصدق في الأصل مطلقًا سواء كان بحسب الصدق في كل فرد أو بحسب الصدق في البعض منها حتى إذا كان مشتملا على الصدق بوجه ما فإنه ينعكس عكسا صادقا والاتحاد في العكس كلية كانت القضية أو جزئية يدل على هذا دلالة ظاهرة تعرف بالتأمل. وأيضًا قولهم مع بقاء الصدق والكذب بحاله يدل على انحصار الأصل في كونه صادقًا أو كاذبا ؛ والانحصار لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون المراد من كونه
As for the second investigation, it is concerning simple conversion. As for conversion, it is one of the necessary concomitants of the proposition, as has passed previously, except that it is called by this name among them because it is according to the change of the parts or according to what corresponds to each one of them. The first of them is simple conversion, and the majority of philosophers have defined it as making the subject a predicate and the predicate a subject while preserving the negation and affirmation as they are; and truth and falsehood as they are also, except that this is a definition particular to categoricals, since simple conversion in conditionals is remote from it. The truth in it is that one should say it is the replacement of the whole thing judged about (al-mahkum 'alayh) with the thing judged by (al-mahkum bih), and the thing judged by with the thing judged about, while preserving negation and affirmation as they are, and truth and falsehood as they are likewise in all materials. Then a speaker may say: How is that, when there are false propositions whose converse is true? For example, it is said: Everything existent is possible, and despite its falsehood, it converts to a true converse, which is our saying: Some possible is existent. But we say: Although it is false, it includes what is true in itself, which is our saying: Some existent is possible. So that converse, inasmuch as it is true, is the converse of this true [proposition], not the converse of that false one, unless it includes that [truth] or we do not know it; so we cannot say that it converts to a true converse. And if it is said: If it were as you mentioned, then the converse in something like our saying: Every human is an animal would be the converse of what is within it, which is our saying: Some human is an animal, not the converse of that [universal]. So we say: Granted that it is so, but why did you say that it is not possible? Rather, it is possible that the consideration of truth in the converse is according to the truth in the original absolutely, whether according to truth in every individual or according to truth in some of them. So if it includes truth in some way, then it converts to a true converse. And the identity in conversion, whether the proposition is universal or particular, indicates this with a clear indication known by reflection. And also, their saying 'with preserving truth and falsehood as they are' indicates the restriction of the original to being true or false; and restriction is not possible except that the intended meaning of it...
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion ('aks mustawi) as an immediate inference where the subject and predicate are swapped while preserving the quality (affirmation/negation) and truth value. The author first gives the standard definition of the majority: making the subject a predicate and vice versa, but notes this is specific to categoricals; for conditionals, conversion is different. Then he provides his own definition: the whole thing judged about (the subject) becomes the thing judged by (the predicate), and the whole thing judged by becomes the thing judged about, preserving negation/affirmation and truth/falsehood in all materials (i.e., regardless of content). An objection is then raised: some false propositions have true converses, e.g., 'Everything existent is possible' is false, but its converse 'Some possible is existent' is true. The author replies that the false original includes a true proposition within it ('Some existent is possible'), and the true converse is actually the converse of that underlying true proposition, not the false one. Another objection: if we take 'Every human is an animal', its converse should be the converse of 'Some human is an animal' (which is true) rather than the original universal. The author responds that this is acceptable; truth in the original can be considered absolutely, whether per se or per accidens. The definition's requirement that truth and falsehood remain the same means the original must be either true or false; the converse will have the same truth status as the original (or as the underlying truth).

Definition of 'Aks Mustawi and Resolution of the False Premise Objection
Author defines conversion and resolves an objection about false premises yielding true converses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple conversion ('aks mustawi) and explain why a false proposition cannot directly yield a true converse without containing a true sub-proposition.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate; a false premise yields a true converse only if it contains a true part.

The author defines 'aks mustawi as swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth. However, a common objection arises: sometimes a false premise seems to have a true converse. The author resolves this by showing that the truth must already be implicitly present in the original.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard definition
Most philosophers define 'aks mustawi as making the subject the predicate and the predicate the subject while preserving affirmation/negation and truth value.
This definition is specific to categorical propositions.
2
Note the author's refined definition
The author adds 'the exchange of that which is judged upon entirely for that which is judged by' — meaning the entire subject and entire predicate swap places, emphasizing completeness.
This refinement ensures no part is left out.
3
Analyze the objection example
The objector says: 'Every existent is possible' is false, yet its converse 'Some possible is existent' is true.
This seems to violate the rule that conversion preserves truth.
4
Trace the author's resolution
The author argues that the false premise contains a true sub-proposition 'Some existent is possible'. The true converse is the converse of this true part, not of the false whole.
Thus conversion still preserves truth relative to the true part.
5
Consider the implications for truth preservation
The rule holds if truth is considered 'absolutely' — whether it applies to every individual or only some. A proposition may be partially true, and conversion preserves that partial truth.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

The alteration of parts or according to what corresponds to each one of them. The first of the two is 'aks mustawi (the simple converse). The majority of philosophers defined it as making the subject predicate and the predicate subject while preserving the negation and affirmation in their state, and truth and falsehood also in their state — except that this is the definition specific to categoricals; for simple conversion in conditionals is remote from it. The truth about it is that it is said: it is the exchange of that which is judged upon entirely for that which is judged by, and that which is judged by for that which is judged upon, while preserving the negation and affirmation in their state, and truth and falsehood in their state likewise in all matters. Then a speaker may say: How is that when there are false propositions whose converse is true? For example, 'Every existent is possible' — even though it is false, its converse is true, namely 'Some possible is existent'. But we say: Although it is false, it contained something true in itself, namely 'Some existent is possible'. So that converse, insofar as it is true, is the converse of this true [part], not the converse of that false [proposition] — unless it contains that [true part] or we do not know that it contains it. Thus we cannot say that it yields a true converse. And if it is said: If it were as you mentioned, then the converse in something like our saying 'Every human is an animal' would be the converse of what is included in it, namely 'Some human is an animal', not the converse of that [original]. We say: Granted it is so, but why do you say it is impossible? Rather it is possible that the consideration of truth in the converse is by the consideration of truth in the original absolutely, whether it is according to truth in every individual or according to truth in some of them, such that if it contains truth in some respect, then it yields a true converse. The union in the converse, whether the proposition is universal or particular, indicates this clearly, known by reflection. Also their statement 'while preserving truth and falsehood in their state' indicates the restriction of the original to being either true or false. Restriction is only possible if the intended meaning of its being true is that it contains truth absolutely, as before; for truth in the original may be according to truth in every individual, or it may not be; falsehood in that may be according to falsehood in every individual, or it may not be, so it has three divisions. This contradicts the restriction to containing either truth or falsehood. And their statement indicates its being restricted. And some of those who claimed that falsehood in the original is among what is considered said: 'Some possible is existent', for example, is not the converse of 'Every existent is possible', but rather its inversion (qalb). That is a corrupt statement, for it has no basis at all; inversion cannot be in the form of conversion, for it is the alteration of parts without the parts being exchanged from their positions, contrary to conversion.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage defines 'aks mustawi (simple conversion) and addresses an objection about false premises. The author begins by stating that conversion involves swapping parts, and the most common type is 'aks mustawi, defined by most philosophers as swapping subject and predicate while preserving affirmation/negation and truth value. However, this definition is specific to categorical propositions, not conditionals. The author then gives a refined definition: swapping the entire subject and predicate positions while maintaining quantity? Actually it says 'the exchange of that which is judged upon entirely for that which is judged by'—this means replacing the subject with the predicate and vice versa. The objection arises: sometimes a false proposition has a true converse, e.g., 'Every existent is possible' (false) converts to 'Some possible is existent' (true). The author responds by noting that the false premise contains a true sub-proposition ('Some existent is possible'), and it is that true part which converses to the true converse. The true converse is the converse of the true part, not of the false original. But if the false premise does not contain any truth, then its converse may be false, so the rule remains: conversion preserves truth only when the original has some truth. The author then discusses the notion of 'containing truth absolutely'—truth may be universal or particular, and falsehood may be universal or particular, giving three cases. The standard definition's phrase 'while preserving truth and falsehood' requires the original to be either wholly true or wholly false, but in reality a proposition might be partially true. The author resolves this by saying truth consideration is absolute. Finally, the author refutes the claim that the converse of a universal affirmative is the inversion (qalb) rather than conversion, stating that inversion does not swap positions like conversion. This passage is a detailed expansion on the definition and conditions of conversion.

Converting Affirmative External Propositions
Author explains conversion rule for affirmative external propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for affirmative external propositions to derive the correct converse and its modality.

Core Idea
The converse of an affirmative external is always particular affirmative with absolute generality.

This passage from the logic text explains a key conversion rule for propositions whose truth depends on external existence (khariji). The author shows that regardless of whether the original is universal or particular, necessary or not, the converse must be a particular affirmative with the modality of absolute generality (itlaq 'amm). This rule prevents the common mistake of thinking 'every human is animal' converts to 'every animal is human'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition type
Check if the proposition is affirmative and external (mujaba kharijiyya): the predicate holds for actual instances of the subject. Examples: 'Every human is animal', 'Necessarily every A is B'.
External means existence in the outside world, not just essence.
2
Apply the universal rule
The converse is always a particular affirmative (جزئية) and has the modality of absolute generality (الإطلاق العام). Write the converse as 'Some predicate is subject' with 'at some time' implied.
Absolute generality means the proposition holds at least at some time, not necessarily always.
3
Justify particularity
The predicate may be more general than the subject, so you cannot predicate the subject of every predicate instance. Example: 'Every human is animal' – 'animal' is broader, so only 'Some animal is human' is valid.
This is why the converse is not universal.
4
Justify the modality
If the converse were false with a stronger modality (like perpetual), its contradictory would be true, leading to a contradiction with the original proposition. The author illustrates this with the necessary universal affirmative: if 'Necessarily every A is B' is true, then 'Some B is A' must be true at least sometimes, otherwise 'No B is A eternally' would be true, contradicting the original.
This shows why absolute generality is the correct modality.
5
Practice with an Islamic example
Convert 'Every intoxicating drink is haram' (assuming universal affirmative external). The converse is 'Some haram thing is an intoxicating drink' (particular affirmative with absolute generality). Check that the predicate 'haram thing' is broader.
This conversion is used in fiqh analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العكس المستوي يجري في جميع الموجبات، وفي البعض من السوالب كذلك سواء كان الموضوع في كل واحدة منهما بحسب الحقيقية أو بحسب الخارج ؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد. أما في الموجبات الخارجية فالموجبة إذا كانت فعليته كلية كانت أو جزئية ضرورية كانت أو لا ضرورية فعكسها موجبة جزئية موجهة بجهة الإطلاق العام. أما أنه موجبة جزئية فلاحتمال أن يكون المحكوم [به] أعم من المحكوم عليه كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان مثلا فلا يمكن أن يحمل الموضوع على كل واحد من المحمول. وأما أنه موجهة بجهة الإطلاق العام فلاستلزام نقيضها المحال بانعكاس النقيض تارةً، وانضمامه إلى الأصل أخرى. أما بالانعكاس فذلك في مثل قولنا بالضرورة كل «أب»، فإنه إذا صدق هذا صدق قولنا بعض ب أ » بالإطلاق العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا لا شيء من ب أ دائما. والسالبة الكلية الدائمة تنعكس كنفسها
Then simple conversion applies to all affirmatives, and to some negatives as well, whether the subject in each of them is according to reality (haqiqiyya) or according to externals (khariji). That will be known later. As for affirmative external propositions, if the affirmative is actual, whether universal or particular, necessary or not necessary, its converse is a particular affirmative with the modality of absolute generality. As for its being a particular affirmative, it is because the predicate may be more general than the subject, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal', for example, so the subject cannot be predicated of every individual of the predicate. And as for its being with the modality of absolute generality, it is because the contradictory of its contradictory entails absurdity by conversion of the contradictory sometimes and by its conjunction with the original at other times. As for by conversion, that is like our saying 'Necessarily every A is B'; for if this is true, then our saying 'Some B is A' is true by absolute generality, otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'No B is A eternally'. And the perpetual universal negative converts as itself.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage discusses the application of simple conversion to affirmative propositions, specifically those called 'external' (khariji). The author first states that conversion applies to all affirmatives and to some negatives, regardless of whether the subject is considered in terms of its essence (haqiqiyya) or its external instances (khariji). This will be clarified later. Focusing on affirmative external propositions: if the affirmative is actual (i.e., the predicate holds in the external world), whether universal or particular, necessary or not, its converse is a particular affirmative with the modality of absolute generality (itlaq 'amm). Why particular? Because the predicate may be more general than the subject, as in 'Every human is animal' — you cannot predicate 'human' of every animal. Why absolute generality? Because if the converse were false, its contradictory (a universal negative) would be true, leading to absurdity. The author gives an example: 'Necessarily every A is B' — if true, then 'Some B is A' is true (absolutely), for if not, then 'No B is A eternally' would be true, which contradicts the original. The last line mentions that the perpetual universal negative (saliba kulliyya da'ima) converts as itself (i.e., to a universal negative). This passage is a technical explanation of the conversion rules for affirmative propositions, with a justification for the particularity and modality of the converse.

Key Terms
الموجبة الخارجية
الإطلاق العام

Key Takeaway
The conversion of an affirmative external proposition yields a particular affirmative with absolute generality, never a universal.

Conversion of Affirmative Categorical Propositions and Its Proofs
Author explains the conversion rule for affirmative categorical propositions with two proof methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the converse of any affirmative categorical proposition according to the rule of 'aks mustawi and reconstruct the two logical proofs that justify the rule.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative converts to particular affirmative; the proofs use contradiction to show the converse must hold.

In classical Islamic logic, immediate inference rules are essential for transforming propositions. One key rule is simple conversion ('aks mustawi) for affirmative propositions. This passage explains the rule and provides two rigorous proofs: by contraposition and by composition. Understanding these proofs deepens your grasp of logical necessity and the relationship between subject and predicate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the proposition is universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I). Note its modality (necessary, actual, possible). In the text, actual affirmatives are handled first, then possibles.
2
Apply the conversion rule
Swap the subject and predicate. For a universal affirmative, change the quantity to particular. Keep the quality as affirmative. Set the modality to 'absolute generality' for actual propositions or 'absolute possibility' for possibles. For example, 'Every A is B necessarily' becomes 'Some B is A with absolute generality'.
The converse cannot be universal because the predicate may be more general than the subject.
3
Reconstruct the contraposition proof
Assume the converse is false, so its contradictory 'No B is A always' is true. Combine this with the original 'Every A is B necessarily'. Since the universal negative 'No A is B always' follows from 'No B is A always' (by conversion of E), we get a contradiction with the original. Therefore the converse must be true.
This proof relies on the convertibility of the universal negative.
4
Reconstruct the composition proof
Assume the converse is false, so 'No B is A' is true. Combine with 'Every A is B necessarily' to form a first-figure syllogism: Every A is B, No B is A, therefore No A is A. This self-divestiture is impossible, so the assumption is false and the converse must be true.
The author also addresses objections about the middle term and the truth of premises.
5
Evaluate objections and extensions
The author notes that the contraposition method requires the universal negative to be convertible, which is not always the case, and postpones discussion. For composition, he explains that the syllogism works only in the first figure. He also clarifies that the impossibility arises only if premises are true in reality, not hypothetically. Finally, for possible propositions, the converse takes absolute possibility.

فيضم هذا إلى الأصل ويقال بالضرورة كل أب»، ولا شيء من ب أ) دائما فلا شيء من «أ أ» دائما، هذا خلف لاستحالة انسلاب الشيء عن نفسه. ولا يبعد أن يقال في طريق الانعكاس إنه لا يتم إلا وأن تكون السالبة الكلية منعكسة; وفيه من الكلام فإن من السوالب الكلية ما لا يمكن انعكاسه. ومن الدلائل العقلية ما يدل على امتناع العكس فيها كذلك على ما سيجيء من بعد. لكنا نقول : هذا من جملة ما يكون من اللوازم ذكره من بعد فيذكر في موضعه ويعرف إن شاء الله تعالى. وأما في طريق الانضمام فيقال أيضًا إنما يلزم منه لا شيء من «أ أن دائما إذا كان الحد الأوسط فيه هو الباء فأما إذا لم يكن فلا. لكنا نقول: هذا القياس إما أن يكون من الشكل الأول وحينئذ يلزم منه أن لا يكون شيء من «أ أ» دائما كما مر ، وهذا ظاهر، وإما أن يكون من الشكل الرابع وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يكون بعض ب ب، وإنه من جملة المتخيلات أيضا على نحو ذلك. وإن قيل هب أن ما ذكرتم يتم أولا وثانيًا لكن يلزم منه أن الواقع وهو المركب من مقدمتين صادقتين مستلزم للمحال. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك فنقول: هذا من المسلمات إذا كان / المركب من مقدمتين صادقتين بالحقيقية. فأما إذا كان بالفرض فلا وإنه كذلك، هذا إذا كانت الموجبة من الفعليات. فأما إذا كانت من الممكنات فالممكنة كلية كانت أو جزئية فعكسها جزئية موجهة بجهة الإمكان العام فيقال مثلا كل «أب» بالإمكان العام، بعض ب أ)
Then simple conversion runs in all affirmatives, and in some negatives as well, whether the subject in each of them is according to reality or according to external existence; and that will be known later. As for external affirmatives, if the affirmative is actual, universal or particular, necessary or not necessary, its converse is a particular affirmative directed with the modality of absolute generality. As for it being particular affirmative, it is because the predicate may be more general than the subject, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal' for example, so the subject cannot be predicated of every individual of the predicate. And as for it being directed with the modality of absolute generality, it is due to the impossibility of its contradictory entailing a contradiction, by contraposition (in'ikas al-naqid) once, and by its attachment to the original another time. As for by contraposition, that is in like our saying 'Every A is B necessarily', for if this is true then our saying 'Some B is A' with absolute generality is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying 'No B is A always'. And the universal negative permanent converts like itself, which is our saying 'No A is B always'; and we had 'Every A is B necessarily', this is a contradiction because the conjunction of the two entails the conjunction of contradictories. As for by composition, that is in like our saying 'Every A is B necessarily' also, for if the converse, which is our saying 'Some B is A' with absolute generality, is not true, its contradictory is true, which is our saying 'No B is A'; then this is attached to the original and it is said 'Every A is B necessarily', and 'No B is A always', so 'No A is A always', this is a contradiction due to the impossibility of a thing being divested of itself. And it is not far to say regarding the method of contraposition that it is not complete unless the universal negative is convertible; and there is discussion in it, for among universal negatives are those whose conversion is not possible. And among intellectual evidences is that which indicates the impossibility of conversion in them as will come later. But we say: This is among the things whose mention is necessary later, so it will be mentioned in its place and will be known, God willing. And as for the method of composition, it is also said that from it nothing follows except 'No A is A always' if the middle term is B; but if not, then no. But we say: This syllogism is either from the first figure, and then from it follows that nothing is 'No A is A always' as has passed, and this is apparent; or it is from the fourth figure, and then it follows that some B is not B, and it is among the imaginary things as well in that manner. And if it is said: Grant that what you have mentioned is valid firstly and secondly, but from it follows that the actual, which is composed of two true premises, entails an impossibility. And it is impossible for it to be so. Then we say: This is among the conceded things if the composite is of two true premises according to reality. As for if it is hypothetically, then no, and it is so. This is if the affirmative is from actuals. As for if it is from possibles, then the possible, universal or particular, its converse is particular directed with the modality of absolute possibility. So it is said for example 'Every A is B with absolute possibility, some B is A'.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the conversion (عكس مستوي) of affirmative categorical propositions (الموجبات الحمليات). The author begins by stating that simple conversion applies to all affirmatives and to some negatives, whether the subject is taken according to real essence (بحسب الحقيقية) or according to external existence (بحسب الخارج), a point to be clarified later. Focus then shifts to external affirmatives: any actual affirmative, universal or particular, necessary or not, converts to a particular affirmative with the modality of absolute generality (الإطلاق العام). The reason it is particular is that the predicate (محمول) may be broader than the subject (موضوع), as in 'Every human is an animal'—you cannot say 'Every animal is human'. The reason it has absolute generality is proven via two methods: contraposition (انعكاس النقيض) and composition (انضمام). 

The contraposition method: take 'Every A is B necessarily'. If true, then 'Some B is A' with absolute generality must be true; otherwise its contradictory 'No B is A always' would be true. But if 'Every A is B necessarily' and 'No B is A always' are both true, they entail a contradiction because the first implies some B is A at some time, while the second denies it always. The universal negative always converts to itself (e.g., 'No A is B always' converts to 'No B is A always'). 

The composition method: again with 'Every A is B necessarily'. Assume the converse 'Some B is A' with absolute generality is false, so its contradictory 'No B is A' is true. Combine this with the original premise: 'Every A is B necessarily' and 'No B is A always' yield 'No A is A always', an impossibility (self-divestiture). 

The author then addresses a potential objection: the contraposition method requires that the universal negative be convertible, but some universal negatives are not convertible. He postpones this discussion. For the composition method, an objection arises: the conclusion 'No A is A always' follows only if the middle term is B; otherwise not. The author replies that the syllogism used is either first figure (producing 'No A is A always') or fourth figure (producing 'Some B is not B'), the latter being imaginary. Another objection: if the premises are true, the composite leads to impossibility, but the author clarifies this holds only if the premises are true in reality (حقيقية), not if hypothetically assumed. Finally, for possible propositions (الممكنات), the conversion yields a particular affirmative with absolute possibility. Example: 'Every A is B with absolute possibility' converts to 'Some B is A' (with absolute possibility).

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الإطلاق العام
انعكاس النقيض
الانضمام

Universal affirmative propositions convert only to particular affirmatives with absolute generality, proven by contraposition and composition to avoid logical contradiction.

Proving Conversion by Reductio and Composition
Author explains conversion by reductio and composition for necessary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the reductio and composition methods to derive the converse of a necessary universal affirmative proposition.

Core Idea
Conversion of necessary propositions is proven by deriving an impossibility from denying the converse.

The author presents two methods for establishing the converse of a necessary proposition: reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) and composition (indimām). These are foundational for immediate inference in Islamic logic. We will walk through each method step by step using the Arabic variables given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its desired converse
Begin with a necessary universal affirmative: 'Every A is B necessarily'. The desired converse is 'Some B is A' (with absolute generality, i.e., without necessity).
The original is a universal affirmative (Aabī) with necessity; the converse is a particular affirmative (Bajī).
2
Apply reductio: assume the converse is false
Assume the converse 'Some B is A' is false. This means its contradictory 'No B is A' is true. Combine this with the original 'Every A is B' to get 'No A is A' (contradiction). Since the combination yields an impossibility, the converse must be true.
The contradiction is that a thing cannot be stripped of itself (lā shay'a min 'a' dā'iman).
3
Apply composition: use the contradictory of the false converse
If the converse is false, take its contradictory 'No B is A' (a universal negative). Combine it with the original 'Every A is B' to form a categorical syllogism: major premise 'No B is A', minor premise 'Every A is B', conclusion 'No A is A'. This impossibility proves the converse cannot be false.
This syllogism is in the first figure (Celarent mood) with middle term B.
4
Understand the role of universal negative conversion
The composition method assumes that the universal negative 'No B is A' is convertible to 'No A is B' (otherwise the argument might fail). The author notes that not all universal negatives are convertible, a point reserved for later.
This shows that the method is not universally applicable without conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأما في طريق الانضمام فيقال أيضًا إنما يلزم منه لا شيء من «أ أن دائما إذا كان الحد الأوسط فيه هو الباء فأما إذا لم يكن فلا. لكنا نقول: هذا القياس إما أن يكون من الشكل الأول وحينئذ يلزم منه أن لا يكون شيء من «أ أ» دائما كما مر ، وهذا ظاهر، وإما أن يكون من الشكل الرابع وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يكون بعض ب ب، وإنه من جملة المتخيلات أيضا على نحو ذلك. وإن قيل هب أن ما ذكرتم يتم أولا وثانيًا لكن يلزم منه أن الواقع وهو المركب من مقدمتين صادقتين مستلزم للمحال. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك فنقول: هذا من المسلمات إذا كان المركب من مقدمتين صادقتين بالحقيقية. فأما إذا كان بالفرض فلا وإنه كذلك، هذا إذا كانت الموجبة من الفعليات. فأما إذا كانت من الممكنات فالممكنة كلية كانت أو جزئية فعكسها جزئية موجهة بجهة الإمكان العام فيقال مثلا كل «أب» بالإمكان العام، بعض ب أ) بالإمكان العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا لا واحد من ب أ) بالضرورة وإنه لا يصدق إلا وأن يصدق قولنا لا واحد من أب» بالضرورة، وقد كان كل أب بالإمكان الخاص، هذا خلف. ثمَّ النقيض يضم الأصل أيضًا ويقال كل «أب» بالإمكان الخاص ، لا واحد من ب أ ) بالضرورة فلا واحد من «أ أ» بالضرورة.
And it is our saying: 'No A is B always, and every A was necessarily B'; this is a contradiction because the combination of them necessitates the combination of the two contradictories. As for the method of composition, it is in such as our saying 'necessarily every A is B' also, for if its converse, which is our saying 'some B is A' with absolute generality, is not true, its contradictory, which is our saying 'no B is A', is true. So this is combined with the original and it is said: 'necessarily every A is B', and 'no B is A always', so 'no A is A always', which is a contradiction because it is impossible for a thing to be stripped of itself. And it is not improbable that it is said concerning the method of conversion that it is not completed unless the universal negative is convertible; and there is discussion in it, for among universal negatives there are those whose conversion is impossible. And from intellectual proofs what indicates the impossibility of their conversion likewise, as will come later. But we say: this is among the things it is necessary to mention later; it will be mentioned in its place and known, God willing.
As for the method of composition, it is also said that it only necessitates 'no A is A always' if the middle term is B; but if not, then not. But we say: this syllogism is either from the first figure, in which case it necessitates that nothing is A always as passed, and this is apparent; or from the fourth figure, in which case it necessitates that some B is not B, and that is also among the imaginary things in that manner. And if it is said: suppose that what you mention is completed firstly and secondly, but it necessitates that the reality, which is composed of two true premises, entails an impossibility. And it is impossible that it be so. Then we say: this is among accepted things if the composite of two true premises is real. But if it is hypothetical, then no, and it is so. This is when the affirmative is from actualities. As for when it is from possibles, the possible, whether universal or particular, its converse is particular qualified with the modality of general possibility. So it is said, for example: 'every A is B with general possibility', 'some B is A with general possibility'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying 'no B is A necessarily', and it is not true unless our saying 'no A is B necessarily' is true, and every A is B with special possibility; this is a contradiction. Then the contradictory is also combined with the original, and it is said: 'every A is B with special possibility', 'no B is A necessarily', so 'no A is A necessarily'.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage explains two methods for converting categorical propositions: (1) the method of reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) and (2) the method of composition (indimam). The author begins by illustrating the reductio method: if we have 'No A is B always' and 'Every A is necessarily B', the conjunction implies a contradiction because it would combine contradictories. This shows the necessity of the converse. The composition method is then introduced: starting with 'necessarily every A is B', if the converse 'some B is A' with absolute generality is false, its contradictory 'no B is A' is true. Combining this with the original yields 'no A is A'—an impossibility, thus validating the converse. The author notes that some universal negatives are not convertible, a topic reserved for later. Then the composition method is scrutinized: it requires that the middle term be B in a first-figure syllogism to derive 'no A is A'; otherwise, if the middle term is in the fourth figure, it yields 'some B is not B', which is considered imaginary. The author addresses an objection: if two true premises lead to an impossibility, the premises cannot both be true. The response distinguishes real vs. hypothetical truth. Finally, the conversion of possible propositions is discussed: a universal or particular possible affirmative converts to a particular possible affirmative with general possibility, demonstrated by an indirect proof involving special possibility. The explanation follows the author's sequential argumentation, preserving the technical precision of each step.

Key Terms
انعكاس (in‘ikās)
خلف (khalaf)
انضمام (indimām)
إطلاق عام (iṭlāq ‘āmm)

The conversion of necessary universal affirmatives is proven by showing that denying the converse leads to a logical impossibility, using either reductio or composition methods.

Conversion of Possible Propositions
Author explains conversion of possible propositions using reductio with modal distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a universal possible affirmative proposition into its particular possible converse using the reductio method and identify the required modality.

Core Idea
Possible propositions convert to particular possible propositions under general possibility, not necessity.

After handling necessary propositions, the author addresses possible (mumkin) propositions. The conversion of a possible proposition yields a particular affirmative qualified by general possibility (imkān ‘āmm). This lesson walks through the reductio proof as given in the text.

Guided Path
1
Restate the original possible proposition
Start with a universal possible affirmative: 'Every A is B with general possibility' (imkān ‘āmm). Note that general possibility means both existence and non-existence are possible.
The original uses 'imkān ‘āmm' for the premise.
2
Assume the converse is false
The desired converse is 'Some B is A with general possibility'. Assume it is false. Then its contradictory, 'No B is A necessarily' (salba darūriyya), is true.
The contradictory of a particular possible affirmative is a universal negative necessary proposition, because the negation of 'some B is A possibly' is 'no B is A necessarily'.
3
Derive a necessary universal negative from the assumption
From 'No B is A necessarily', we can infer 'No A is B necessarily' by simple conversion of universal negative (valid for necessary propositions). This step is implicit in the text.
The text says: 'it is not true unless our saying 'no A is B necessarily' is true'.
4
Combine with the original to get an impossibility
We now have 'Every A is B with special possibility' (the original) and 'No B is A necessarily' (or equivalently 'No A is B necessarily'). Combining them yields 'No A is A necessarily', which is impossible. Hence the converse cannot be false.
Special possibility (imkān khāṣṣ) is a stronger modality than general possibility; the original premise must be assumed with special possibility for the reductio to work.
5
Conclude the converse's validity and modality
Since the assumption leads to absurdity, the converse 'Some B is A with general possibility' must be true. The converse is particular because conversion of a universal possible yields a particular possible (by the rules of conversion).
Thus the conversion of a universal possible affirmative is a particular possible affirmative with general possibility.

Source Text

وهو قولنا لا شيء من «أب» دائمًا؛ وقد كان كل «أب» بالضرورة، هذا خلف لما أن الاجتماع بينهما مما يستلزم الاجتماع بين النقيضين. وأما بالانضمام فذلك في مثل قولنا بالضرورة كل «أب» أيضًا فإنه إذا لم يصدق العكس وهو قولنا بعض ب أ) بالإطلاق العام يصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا لا شيء من أب أ»؛ فيضم هذا إلى الأصل ويقال بالضرورة كل أب»، ولا شيء من ب أ) دائما فلا شيء من «أ أ» دائما، هذا خلف لاستحالة انسلاب الشيء عن نفسه. ولا يبعد أن يقال في طريق الانعكاس إنه لا يتم إلا وأن تكون السالبة الكلية منعكسة؛ وفيه من الكلام فإن من السوالب الكلية ما لا يمكن انعكاسه. ومن الدلائل العقلية ما يدل على امتناع العكس فيها كذلك على ما سيجيء من بعد. لكنا نقول : هذا من جملة ما يكون من اللوازم ذكره من بعد فيذكر في موضعه ويعرف إن شاء الله تعالى.

And it is our saying: 'No A is B always, and every A was necessarily B'; this is a contradiction because the combination of them necessitates the combination of the two contradictories. As for the method of composition, it is in such as our saying 'necessarily every A is B' also, for if its converse, which is our saying 'some B is A' with absolute generality, is not true, its contradictory, which is our saying 'no B is A', is true. So this is combined with the original and it is said: 'necessarily every A is B', and 'no B is A always', so 'no A is A always', which is a contradiction because it is impossible for a thing to be stripped of itself. And it is not improbable that it is said concerning the method of conversion that it is not completed unless the universal negative is convertible; and there is discussion in it, for among universal negatives there are those whose conversion is impossible. And from intellectual proofs what indicates the impossibility of their conversion likewise, as will come later. But we say: this is among the things it is necessary to mention later; it will be mentioned in its place and known, God willing.
As for the method of composition, it is also said that it only necessitates 'no A is A always' if the middle term is B; but if not, then not. But we say: this syllogism is either from the first figure, in which case it necessitates that nothing is A always as passed, and this is apparent; or from the fourth figure, in which case it necessitates that some B is not B, and that is also among the imaginary things in that manner. And if it is said: suppose that what you mention is completed firstly and secondly, but it necessitates that the reality, which is composed of two true premises, entails an impossibility. And it is impossible that it be so. Then we say: this is among accepted things if the composite of two true premises is real. But if it is hypothetical, then no, and it is so. This is when the affirmative is from actualities. As for when it is from possibles, the possible, whether universal or particular, its converse is particular qualified with the modality of general possibility. So it is said, for example: 'every A is B with general possibility', 'some B is A with general possibility'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying 'no B is A necessarily', and it is not true unless our saying 'no A is B necessarily' is true, and every A is B with special possibility; this is a contradiction. Then the contradictory is also combined with the original, and it is said: 'every A is B with special possibility', 'no B is A necessarily', so 'no A is A necessarily'.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage explains two methods for converting categorical propositions: (1) the method of reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) and (2) the method of composition (indimam). The author begins by illustrating the reductio method: if we have 'No A is B always' and 'Every A is necessarily B', the conjunction implies a contradiction because it would combine contradictories. This shows the necessity of the converse. The composition method is then introduced: starting with 'necessarily every A is B', if the converse 'some B is A' with absolute generality is false, its contradictory 'no B is A' is true. Combining this with the original yields 'no A is A'—an impossibility, thus validating the converse. The author notes that some universal negatives are not convertible, a topic reserved for later. Then the composition method is scrutinized: it requires that the middle term be B in a first-figure syllogism to derive 'no A is A'; otherwise, if the middle term is in the fourth figure, it yields 'some B is not B', which is considered imaginary. The author addresses an objection: if two true premises lead to an impossibility, the premises cannot both be true. The response distinguishes real vs. hypothetical truth. Finally, the conversion of possible propositions is discussed: a universal or particular possible affirmative converts to a particular possible affirmative with general possibility, demonstrated by an indirect proof involving special possibility. The explanation follows the author's sequential argumentation, preserving the technical precision of each step.

Key Terms
إمكان عام (imkān ‘āmm)
إمكان خاص (imkān khāṣṣ)
فعليات (fi‘liyyāt)
ممكنات (mumkināt)

D) Absolute generality (iṭlāq ‘āmm)

Key Takeaway
Possible propositions convert to particular possible propositions under general possibility, and the proof relies on a reductio that uses special possibility to generate a contradiction.
