Resolving Apparent Counterexamples to Conversion of Affirmatives
Author analyzes problematic affirmative conversions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly convert affirmative propositions that have compound predicates (e.g., 'was a child') and avoid false impressions of inconvertibility.

Core Idea
Compound predicates disguise conversion; always extract the real predicate first.

Conversion rules for universal and particular affirmatives are straightforward in theory, but some examples seem to break them. The author examines cases like 'every adolescent was a child' and 'some peg is in the wall', showing that the apparent failure is due to misidentifying the predicate. Once the predicate is correctly analyzed as a compound, the conversion holds. This lesson teaches you to spot and handle such disguised predicates.

Guided Path
1
Identify subject and predicate
Locate the grammatical subject and predicate in the problematic proposition. In 'every adolescent was a child', the subject is 'adolescent' and the predicate is 'was a child' (not just 'child').
The copula 'was' is part of the predicate.
2
Check if the predicate is a compound
A compound predicate includes additional tense or relation words. If the predicate is a simple noun, convert normally; if it is a compound (e.g., 'was a child', 'in the wall'), treat the whole phrase as the predicate.
3
Apply conversion with the correct predicate
For 'every adolescent was a child', the predicate is 'thing that was a child'. Convert to 'some of what was a child is an adolescent'. This converse is true, resolving the apparent failure.
Always use the full predicate term in the converse.
4
Verify the truth of the converse
Check that the converse is indeed true given the original. For 'some peg is in the wall', the predicate 'in the wall' is relational. The correct converse is 'some of what is in the wall is a peg'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن من القضايا الموجبة ما يوهم أن لا ينعكس مثل قولنا كل متر عرع كان طفلا فإنه يصدق؛ ولا يصدق قولنا بعض الطفل كان متر عرعًا لكنها تنعكس. فإن الطفل من حيث هو الطفل لا يكون محمولا بل المحمول مجموع قولنا كان طفلا، ولو كان كذلك لكان يلزم من صدق قولنا كل مترعرع كان طفلا صدق قولنا بعض ما كان طفلا فهو مترعرع. وعلى هذا في قولنا كل طفل [كان] جنيناً وكل بطيخ كان نزرا مثلا، هذا إذا كانت القضية كلية. وأما الجزئية فهي متعددة أيضًا مثل قولنا بعض الوتد في الحائط فإنه مما يوهم أن لا ينعكس، وفي الحقيقة أنه من جملة ما ينعكس كما يقال بعض ما في الحائط وتد. وعلى هذا في قولنا بعض السكّين في البطيخ وأمثاله، فهذه كلها من جملة ما ذهب إليه المتقدمون من الحكماء. وأما المتقدمون منهم فقد زعم بعضهم أن المطلقة من الموجبات لا تنعكس مطلقة بل المطلقة وغيرها من الموجبات تنعكس ممكنة عامة؛ وذلك لأن المطلقة من الموجبات لا تخلو من أن تكون ضرورية أو لا ضرورية؛ والضرورية منها تنعكس تارةً ضرورية كما يقال بالضرورة كل إنسان ناطق بالضرورة بعض الناطق إنسان، وتنعكس تارة ممكنة خاصة كما يقال بالضرورة كل كاتب إنسان، بعض الإنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص، ولو كان كذلك لكان اللازم في الكل هو المشترك بينهما وهو الإمكان العام. وعلى هذا في غير الضرورية من القضايا الموجهة فإنها
Know that among affirmative propositions are those that give the impression that they are not convertible, such as our saying 'every adolescent was a child', for it is true, and our saying 'some child was an adolescent' is not true, but it does convert. For the child, insofar as it is child, is not a predicate, rather the predicate is the sum of our saying 'was a child'. And if it were so, then from the truth of our saying 'every adolescent was a child' would follow the truth of our saying 'some of what was a child is an adolescent'. And likewise in our saying 'every child was a fetus' and 'every watermelon was a raisin' for example. This is when the proposition is universal. As for the particular, there are also many, like our saying 'some peg is in the wall', for it gives the impression that it does not convert, but in reality it is among those that convert, as it is said 'some of what is in the wall is a peg'. And likewise in our saying 'some knife is in the watermelon' and examples. So all these are among the views of the earlier philosophers. As for the earlier among them, some claimed that the absolute (mutilaqa) among affirmatives does not convert absolutely, rather the absolute and other affirmatives convert as generally possible. That is because the absolute among affirmatives is either necessary or not necessary. The necessary among them sometimes converts as necessary, as it is said 'necessarily every human is rational: necessarily some rational is human', and sometimes converts as specifically possible, as it is said 'necessarily every writer is human: some human is a writer with specific possibility'. And if it were so, then the necessary in all is the common between them, which is general possibility. And likewise for non-necessary modal propositions, they...
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage addresses prima facie counterexamples to conversion rules for affirmative propositions. The author first examines universal affirmatives like 'every adolescent was a child'. The apparent failure of conversion (since 'some child was an adolescent' may be false) is resolved by analyzing the predicate: the predicate is not simply 'child' but the compound 'was a child'. Thus the correct converse is 'some of what was a child is an adolescent', which is true. The same applies to 'every child was a fetus' and 'every watermelon was a raisin'. For particular affirmatives, the example 'some peg is in the wall' converts to 'some of what is in the wall is a peg'. These examples are attributed to earlier philosophers. Then the author discusses the view of some earlier scholars regarding absolute (mutilaqa) affirmative propositions. They held that absolute affirmatives do not convert as absolute; rather, they convert as generally possible. The reasoning: absolute affirmatives are either necessary or non-necessary. The necessary ones sometimes convert as necessary (e.g., 'every human is rational' → 'some rational is human' necessarily) and sometimes as specifically possible (e.g., 'every writer is human' → 'some human is a writer' with specific possibility). The common factor in both conversions is general possibility, which becomes the modality of the converse for absolute affirmatives. The passage ends mid-sentence, noting that the same applies to non-necessary modal propositions.

Modal Conversion of Absolute Affirmatives
Author explains modal conversion of absolute affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert absolute affirmative propositions with the correct modality, identifying the modal qualifier of the converse.

Core Idea
Absolute affirmatives, whether necessary or not, convert as generally possible.

The author reports a view of earlier philosophers on the conversion of absolute (non-modal) affirmative propositions. They held that absolute affirmatives cannot convert as absolute; instead, they convert with a modality of general possibility. This is because an absolute affirmative is either necessary or non-necessary, and each has its own conversion pattern, but the common factor is general possibility. This lesson teaches you to determine the correct modal qualifier for the converse of any absolute affirmative.

Guided Path
1
Recognize an absolute affirmative proposition
An absolute affirmative (قضية مطلقة) is a categorical proposition without explicit modal words like 'necessarily' or 'possibly'. Example: 'every writer is human' (no modal qualifier).
2
Determine if the proposition is necessary or non-necessary
A necessary proposition has an essential predicate (e.g., 'every human is rational': rationality is essential). A non-necessary proposition has an accidental predicate (e.g., 'every writer is human': writing is not essential to being human).
In Islamic logic, necessity of predication is judged by whether the predicate is intrinsic to the subject.
3
Apply conversion based on necessity
If necessary, the converse can be either necessary (if both terms are essential, as in 'every human is rational' -> 'some rational is human' necessarily) or specifically possible (if the predicate is accidental, as in 'every writer is human' -> 'some human is a writer' with specific possibility).
Specific possibility (ممكنة خاصة) excludes necessity.
4
Derive the common modal qualifier
The common denominator between necessary and specific possibility conversions is general possibility (ممكنة عامة). Thus, for any absolute affirmative, the converse can safely be stated as generally possible. For non-necessary absolute affirmatives, the converse is generally possible as well.
The passage states that the necessary in all is the common between them, which is general possibility.
5
State the conversion with general possibility
For 'every writer is human' (absolute), the converse is 'some human is a writer, with general possibility'. This covers both necessary and non-necessary cases.

Apparent Non-Conversion of Affirmative Propositions
Author explains problematic conversion cases and their resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly convert affirmative propositions that appear not to convert by identifying the real predicate and applying the conversion rules.

Core Idea
Conversion depends on the real predicate, not just the surface term.

Some affirmative propositions give the impression that they cannot be converted because the immediate converse seems false. However, when we carefully analyze the predicate—especially when it involves temporal or compound phrases—the conversion turns out to be valid. In this lesson, we will learn how to identify such problematic cases and perform correct conversion using examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I). Note that conversion rules differ: A-type converts to I-type, and I-type converts to I-type.
The passage gives examples of both types.
2
Extract the true predicate
Look beyond the surface predicate. If the predicate contains a temporal auxillary (e.g., 'was') or a compound expression (e.g., 'in the wall'), treat the whole phrase as the predicate. For example, in 'Every adolescent was a child', the predicate is not 'child' alone but 'was a child'.
This is crucial for conversion to preserve truth.
3
Apply conversion rule
For A-type (كل س هو ص)، convert to I-type (بعض ص هو س). For I-type (بعض س هو ص)، convert to I-type (بعض ص هو س). Ensure the predicate is swapped as a whole.
Use the real predicate identified in step 2.
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Take 'Every faqih was a student' (كل فقيه كان متعلماً). The predicate is 'was a student'. Conversion yields 'Some of those who were students is a faqih' (بعض من كان متعلماً فهو فقيه), which is true.
This demonstrates that the original conversion holds.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن من القضايا الموجبة ما يوهم أن لا ينعكس مثل قولنا كل مترعرع كان طفلا فإنه يصدق؛ ولا يصدق قولنا بعض الطفل كان مترعرعًا لكنها تنعكس. فإن الطفل من حيث هو الطفل لا يكون محمولا بل المحمول مجموع قولنا كان طفلا، ولو كان كذلك لكان يلزم من صدق قولنا كل مترعرع كان طفلا صدق قولنا بعض ما كان طفلا فهو مترعرع. وعلى هذا في قولنا كل طفل كان جنيناً وكل بطيخ كان نزرا مثلا، هذا إذا كانت القضية كلية. وأما الجزئية فهي متعددة أيضًا مثل قولنا بعض الوتد في الحائط فإنه مما يوهم أن لا ينعكس، وفي الحقيقة أنه من جملة ما ينعكس كما يقال بعض ما في الحائط وتد. وعلى هذا في قولنا بعض السكّين في البطيخ وأمثاله، فهذه كلها من جملة ما ذهب إليه المتقدمون من الحكماء.
Know that among affirmative propositions there are those that give the impression that they do not convert, such as our statement 'Every adolescent was a child'—it is true; but our statement 'Some child was an adolescent' is not true, yet it does convert. For 'child' insofar as it is child is not a predicate; rather the predicate is the composite of our statement 'was a child'. If that were the case, then from the truth of our statement 'Every adolescent was a child' would follow the truth of our statement 'Some of what was a child is an adolescent'. Similarly in our statement 'Every child was a fetus' and 'Every watermelon was a nazz' for example, this when the proposition is universal. As for the particular, it is also numerous, like our statement 'Some wedge is in the wall'—it gives the impression that it does not convert, but in reality it is among what converts, as is said 'Some of what is in the wall is a wedge'. Similarly in our statement 'Some knife is in the watermelon' and the like. All these are among what the earlier philosophers held.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of a common misunderstanding about conversion ('aks). The author states that some affirmative propositions seem not to convert, but they actually do when properly understood. The example given is 'Every adolescent was a child'. At first glance, the converse 'Some child was an adolescent' seems false because not every child becomes an adolescent. However, the author explains that the predicate in the original is not simply 'child' but the whole phrase 'was a child'. So the correct converse is 'Some of what was a child is an adolescent', which is true. Similarly for 'Every child was a fetus' and 'Every watermelon was a nazz' (perhaps a type of plant). The same applies to particular propositions like 'Some wedge is in the wall' converting to 'Some of what is in the wall is a wedge'. The author attributes these observations to the earlier philosophers.

Ancient View: Absolute Affirmatives Convert as Generally Possible
Author discusses the view of earlier logicians on conversion of absolute affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that absolute affirmative propositions convert as generally possible and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
Absolute affirmatives convert not as absolute but as generally possible.

In this passage, the author presents the view of some earlier logicians that absolute affirmative propositions do not retain their modality upon conversion. Instead, they argued that the common modality among all conversions of affirmatives is general possibility. Let's examine their reasoning and formalize it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The ancients claim that absolute affirmative propositions (e.g., 'Every writer is human') do not convert as absolute (without modality), but rather as generally possible. Locate this claim in the source.
2
Extract the supporting examples
Two examples are given: (1) 'Necessarily every human is rational' converts as necessary. (2) 'Necessarily every writer is human' converts as particularly possible. Notice these are necessary affirmatives, not absolute, yet they are used to infer about absolutes.
3
Identify the key inference
The ancients argue that the common modality between necessary and particular possibility is general possibility. Since conversion applies to all affirmatives (including absolute ones), the converse must be generally possible.
4
Formalize the conversion of an absolute affirmative
Take the absolute proposition 'Every writer is human'. According to the ancients, its converse is 'Some human is a writer by general possibility'. Note that the original is non-modal, but the converse has a modality.
5
Evaluate the argument's validity
Consider whether the reasoning is sound. Does the presence of a common modality in necessary affirmatives guarantee that absolute affirmatives also convert with that modality? Think about the nature of absolute propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتقدمون منهم فقد زعم بعضهم أن المطلقة من الموجبات لا تنعكس مطلقة بل المطلقة وغيرها من الموجبات تنعكس ممكنة عامة؛ وذلك لأن المطلقة من الموجبات لا تخلو من أن تكون ضرورية أو لا ضرورية؛ والضرورية منها تنعكس تارةً ضرورية كما يقال بالضرورة كل إنسان ناطق بالضرورة بعض الناطق إنسان، وتنعكس تارة ممكنة خاصة كما يقال بالضرورة كل كاتب إنسان، بعض الإنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص، ولو كان كذلك لكان اللازم في الكل هو المشترك بينهما وهو الإمكان العام. وعلى هذا في غير الضرورية من القضايا الموجهة فإنها تنعكس تارة ممكنة خاصة، وأخرى غير ممكنة خاصة؛ والمشترك بينهما هو الإمكان العام. ولأن العكس في البعض منها لكانت ممكنة عامة وجب أن يكون في الكل كذلك؛ إذ الموجب لاتصافها بصفة الانعكاس هو نفس القضية الموجبة، وجميع الموجبات في نفس هذا الموجب على السواء. وقد قيل في تحقيق هذا الكلام إن المحكوم عليه في القضية الموجبة لا يخلو من أن يكون مأخوذا بحسب الماهية؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العكس في الكل بالإمكان العام أو لا يكون بل يكون مأخوذا بحسب الوجود الخارجي؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العكس في الممكنات بالإمكان العام، وفيما عدا الممكنات بالإطلاق العام كما مر.
As for the earlier among them, some claimed that the absolute [proposition] among affirmatives does not convert as absolute; rather the absolute and other affirmatives convert as generally possible. That is because the absolute among affirmatives is either necessary or not necessary. The necessary among them converts sometimes as necessary, as is said 'Necessarily every human is rational; necessarily some rational is human', and sometimes converts as particularly possible, as is said 'Necessarily every writer is human; some human is a writer by particular possibility'. If that were the case, then the common [modality] between them—general possibility—would be necessary for all. Similarly, for non-necessary modal propositions, they convert sometimes as particular possibility and sometimes as non-particular possibility; the common between them is general possibility. And because the conversion in some of them is general possibility, it must be so in all; for the cause of their being characterized by the property of conversion is the affirmative proposition itself, and all affirmatives are equal in this cause. It has been said in the investigation of this statement that the subject of judgment in the affirmative proposition is either taken according to essence; then the conversion in all must be by general possibility; or it is not, rather it is taken according to external existence; then the conversion in possibles is by general possibility, and in non-possibles by absolute generality, as previously.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents the view of some earlier logicians (المتقدمون) regarding the conversion of absolute ('mutlaqa') affirmative propositions. They claimed that absolute affirmatives do not convert as absolute (i.e., without a modality), but rather they convert with the modality of general possibility (imkan 'amm). Their reasoning: absolute affirmatives are either necessary or not necessary. Necessary affirmatives like 'Every human is rational' convert sometimes as necessary (to 'Some rational is human necessarily') and sometimes as particular possibility (like 'Every writer is human' converts to 'Some human is a writer by particular possibility'). The common modality between these two conversions is general possibility. The same principle applies to other modal propositions: each converts with a modality, but the common factor is general possibility. Therefore, they argued that all absolute affirmatives must convert as generally possible, because the cause of conversion is the affirmative nature itself, which is the same in all cases. The author then reports a further refinement: if the subject in the affirmative is taken according to its essence, the conversion is by general possibility; if taken according to external existence, then for possible things it is general possibility, and for non-possible things it is absolute generality.

The ancients hold that absolute affirmatives convert as generally possible, based on the common modality seen in the conversion of necessary and non-necessary affirmatives.

Conversion of Absolute Propositions
Author states the correct doctrine that absolute propositions convert as absolute, with proof and defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert absolute categorical propositions and verify the conversion using the proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
Absolute propositions convert as absolute—the proof by contradiction reveals its validity.

This passage settles a key debate: absolute propositions (without temporal modality) can be converted into absolute propositions without added modality. The author provides a proof by contradiction and addresses a subtle objection about the nature of contradictories for absolute statements. Understanding this conversion is crucial for immediate inference in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion claim
The author states that if we say 'Every A is B' (a universal affirmative absolute), it follows that 'Some B is A' (a particular affirmative absolute). This is conversion per accidens.
Conversion per accidens preserves truth from universal to particular, unlike simple conversion which is only valid for universal negatives and particulars.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume 'Some B is A' is false. Then its contradictory (or simply its negation) 'No B is A' is true. The universal negative 'No B is A' converts simply to 'No A is B'. But we originally had 'Every A is B', which contradicts 'No A is B'. Therefore the assumption is false, so 'Some B is A' must be true.
The proof uses the fact that the universal negative is the contradictory of the universal affirmative in standard logic, but here the author acknowledges a nuance regarding absolute propositions.
3
Recognize the conversion rules used
The proof employs two conversion rules: (1) conversion per accidens of the universal affirmative to particular affirmative (the claim being proved), and (2) simple conversion of the universal negative (from 'No B is A' to 'No A is B'). The latter is a known valid immediate inference.
4
Examine the objection
An objector says the proof is not 'genuine' (haqiqi) because, for absolute propositions, the universal negative is not the true contradictory of the particular affirmative, since absolute propositions require specifying time and situation for proper contradiction.
This objection reflects a deeper debate in Islamic logic about the contradictory of absolute propositions.
5
Evaluate the author's response
The author responds that the proof is still correct: if 'Some B is A' is false, then there is no B that is A at any time, which suffices to derive a contradiction with 'Every A is B' via the conversion of the universal negative. The term 'genuine' only matters if it means 'valid'; if it means something else, that is not relevant.
The author affirms the logical soundness regardless of the formal contradictory issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالحق في المطلقة أن تنعكس مطلقة فإنا إذا قلنا كل «أب» لزم أن يكون بعض ب أ). وقد جرت العادة في بيان هذا أنه إن لم يكن بعض ب أ فلا شيء من ( ب أ)؛ وهذا مما ينعكس وهو لا شيء من «أب» كما مرَّ من قبل؛ وقد كان كل «أب»، هذا خلف. فهذا هو البيان المعتاد. ولقائل أن يقول فيه إنه لا يكون بيانًا حقيقيا، وإنما يكون بيانًا حقيقيا إذا كان نقيض الموجبة الجزئية المطلقة هو السالبة الكلية المطلقة؛ وفيه من الكلام فإن المطلقة لا يناقضها من نوعها شيء بل من اللوازم أن يتعين الحال والوقت ولا يتعين الحال والوقت فيما نحن فيه فلا يناقضها إذن لكنا نقول: هذا البيان صحيح وإن كانت المطلقة لا يناقضها من نوعها شيء، فإن كان المراد من الحقيقي أن يكون صحيحًا فهو حقيقي؛ وإن كان المراد منه معنى آخر فذلك موقوف إلى أن يتبين. وأما أنه صحيح فذلك أن القائل إذا كذب في قوله بعض «ب أ» فإنه إنما كذب لأنه لا يوجد بعض ما هو «ب» هو «أ» في وقت من الأوقات
In summary, the truth regarding the absolute [proposition] is that it converts as absolute. For if we say 'Every A is B', it follows that 'Some B is A'. The customary demonstration of this is: if there is not some B that is A, then no B is A; and this [universal negative] converts to 'No A is B', as previously; but we had 'Every A is B' — this is a contradiction. So this is the usual demonstration. One might object that it is not a genuine demonstration, and it would only be genuine if the contradictory of the particular absolute affirmative is the universal absolute negative. There is discussion about this, for the absolute [proposition] has no contradictory of its own type; rather, by its implications, the state and time must be specified, and the state and time are not specified in what we are dealing with, so it does not contradict it. But we say: this demonstration is correct even if the absolute has no contradictory of its own type. If what is meant by genuine is that it is correct, then it is genuine. If something else is meant, that is pending clarification. As for its correctness, that is because if one lies in saying 'Some B is A', he only lies because there does not exist some B that is A at any time.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by asserting the correct view: absolute propositions convert as absolute, i.e., without adding any modality. The author provides a proof by contradiction (khalf). The proof: Assume 'Every A is B' is true. If 'Some B is A' were false, then its contradictory (or simply its negation) 'No B is A' would be true. The universal negative 'No B is A' converts to 'No A is B' (by simple conversion). But 'No A is B' contradicts the original 'Every A is B'. Therefore, 'Some B is A' must be true. The author then addresses an objection: this proof might not be considered 'genuine' (haqiqi) because absolute propositions do not have a formal contradictory within their own type—the contradictory of a particular absolute is not a universal absolute with the same time/situation specification. The author responds that the proof is still correct. If by 'genuine' one means 'valid', then it is genuine. If one means something else, that is not discussed. The correctness is established because the falsity of 'Some B is A' implies the non-existence of any B that is A at any time, which leads to the contradiction.

Conversion of Absolute Propositions: Standard Proof
Author presents and defends the conversion rule for absolute propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform the conversion (عكس) of an absolute universal affirmative proposition to a particular affirmative and prove it by contradiction.

Core Idea
Absolute propositions convert as absolute: 'Every A is B' yields 'Some B is A'.

In this lesson, we examine the conversion rule for absolute categorical propositions. The author states the rule and provides a standard proof by contradiction, showing that if the conversion were false, a contradiction would follow. This proof is foundational for immediate inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
State the conversion rule
Identify the rule: from 'Every A is B' (absolute universal affirmative) we infer 'Some B is A' (absolute particular affirmative). This is the claim to be proven.
2
Set up the contradiction assumption
Assume the conclusion is false: 'Some B is A' is false. Then, by the nature of the absolute (not-temporally qualified), this implies 'No B is A' (universal negative absolute).
In absolute propositions, the contradictory of a particular affirmative is a universal negative.
3
Apply conversion to the universal negative
From 'No B is A', convert to 'No A is B' (since conversion of universal negative preserves truth). This step is justified by a previously established rule.
4
Derive contradiction
We now have both 'Every A is B' (original premise) and 'No A is B' (from conversion). These contradict each other. Therefore, the assumption must be false, and 'Some B is A' is true.
This completes the proof by contradiction (reductio ad absurdum).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالحق في المطلقة أن تنعكس مطلقة فإنا إذا قلنا كل أ ب لزم أن يكون بعض ب أ. وقد جرت العادة في بيان هذا أنه إن لم يكن بعض ب أ فلا شيء من ب أ؛ وهذا مما ينعكس وهو لا شيء من أ ب كما مر من قبل؛ وقد كان كل أ ب، هذا خلف. فهذا هو البيان المعتاد. ولقائل أن يقول فيه إنه لا يكون بيانا حقيقيا، وإنما يكون بيانا حقيقيا إذا كان نقيض الموجبة الجزئية المطلقة هو السالبة الكلية المطلقة؛ وفيه من الكلام فإن المطلقة لا يناقضها من نوعها شيء بل من اللوازم أن يتعين الحال والوقت ولا يتعين الحال والوقت فيما نحن فيه فلا يناقضها إذن لكنا نقول: هذا البيان صحيح وإن كانت المطلقة لا يناقضها من نوعها شيء، فإن كان المراد من الحقيقي أن يكون صحيحا فهو حقيقي؛ وإن كان المراد منه معنى آخر فذلك موقوف إلى أن يتبين. وأما أنه صحيح فذلك أن القائل إذا كذب في قوله بعض ب أ فإنه إنما كذب لأنه لا يوجد بعض ما هو ب هو أ في وقت من الأوقات، فإنه إذا وجد يكون صادقا. ولو كان صادقا مع ذلك وهو أن كل ب ليس وقتا ما أ، فليس إذن الكلية السالبة المطلقة تناقض تلك الموجبة الجزئية. ولكن إذا كذبت الجزئية الموجبة صدقت، ولا تكذب تلك الجزئية إلا وأن لا يكون بعض من الأبعاض موصوفا في شيء من الأوقات بالمحمول، فنقيض هذه الجزئية المطلقة إذن هو هذه السالبة التي ظهر من حالها قبل أنها تنعكس كيفيتها؛ وبهذا يظهر أن هذا البيان حق. فإن أحد المطلق على المعنى الأخص لم يكن هذا نقيضه بل يمكن أن يكون كاذبا فليتبين انعكاسه بالافتراض وذلك بأن يقال إذا كان كل أ ب فليفرض واحد من الموصوفات ب أ وليكن ج، فكون ج هو أ وهو ب فالموصوف ب ب الذي هو ج موصوف بأنه أ فيكون بعض ب أ. وإلا فلا شيء من ب أ سلبا مطلقا يعني ما دام ذات ب موصوفة بأنها ب؛ وقد كان كل أ ب فينتج أن لا شيء من أ أ، هذا خلف.
In summary, the correct view regarding the absolute proposition is that it converts as absolute: for if we say 'Every A is B', it follows that 'Some B is A'. The customary demonstration of this is: if not some B is A, then no B is A; this converts to no A is B, as previously shown; but it was given that every A is B; this is a contradiction. So this is the customary demonstration. Someone may object that it is not a genuine demonstration, for a genuine demonstration would require that the contradictory of the particular affirmative absolute is the universal negative absolute. There is discussion about this: the absolute has no contradictory of its own kind, but rather it is necessary to specify the state and time, which are not specified here, so it has no contradictory. But we say: this demonstration is correct even if the absolute has no contradictory of its own kind. If by 'genuine' is meant correct, then it is genuine; if something else is meant, that is suspended until clarified. That it is correct is because if someone says 'Some B is A' and it is false, it is false only because no B is A at any time; if it were found, it would be true. If it were true along with 'Every B is not A at some time', then the universal negative absolute does not contradict that particular affirmative. But when the particular affirmative is false, it is true, and that particular is false only if no part of the parts is described by the predicate at any time; therefore the contradictory of this particular absolute is this negative which was previously shown to convert as to its quality. By this it is clear that this demonstration is correct. As for one who takes absolute in a more specific sense, this is not its contradictory and can be false. Let its conversion be clarified by hypothesis: if every A is B, suppose one of the things described as B is A, let it be C; then C is A and B, so something described as B which is C is described as A, so some B is A. Otherwise, no B is A as an absolute negation, meaning as long as the essence of B is described as B; and it was given that every A is B, so it follows that no A is A; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is an extended treatment of the conversion rule for absolute (مطلقة) categorical propositions. The author begins by stating the rule: 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A'. He then presents the standard proof by contradiction: assume 'Some B is A' is false, i.e., 'No B is A'; this converts to 'No A is B', which contradicts the original 'Every A is B'. The author then addresses an objection: this proof is not genuine because absolute propositions have no true contradictory of their own type (since they lack temporal specification). The author defends the proof by arguing that if 'genuine' means logically correct, it is genuine. He further explains that the contradictory of a particular affirmative absolute is a universal negative absolute that holds precisely when the particular is false. The proof is thus valid. Finally, he provides an alternative proof by hypothesis: choose an individual C that is both A and B, directly showing that some B is A. This second proof is clearer and avoids the earlier subtlety.

Defending the Proof and the Hypothetical Alternative
Author addresses objections and provides an alternative proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the objection against the genuineness of the standard proof and construct an alternative proof by hypothesis (افتراض) for conversion.

Core Idea
A proof can be correct even if not 'genuine' in a strict sense; the hypothetical proof avoids subtlety.

The author anticipates an objection: the standard proof is not a genuine demonstration because absolute propositions lack a true contradictory of their own kind (due to unspecified time). He defends the proof, then offers a cleaner proof by hypothesis that explicitly posits an individual satisfying both terms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims the standard proof is not genuine because for an absolute proposition, the contradictory would require specifying a time, which is absent. Therefore, 'No B is A' might not be the exact contradictory of 'Some B is A'.
Absolute propositions are true if at some time, but false if never. The time factor complicates contradiction.
2
Analyze the author's defense
The author argues: if 'genuine' means logically correct, then the proof is correct. The contradictory of the particular absolute is indeed a universal negative absolute that holds exactly when the particular is false, even without time specification.
The author concedes that if 'genuine' has a stricter meaning, the point is moot until clarified.
3
Examine the proof by hypothesis (iftirad)
To avoid the earlier subtlety, the author proposes a direct proof: if 'Every A is B', suppose there exists an individual C that is B and A. Then C is A and B, so some B is A. If not, then no B is A, leading to a contradiction (No A is A).
This proof assumes a non-empty universe, which is standard in classical logic.
4
Compare the two proofs
The hypothetical proof is simpler and does not rely on the tricky notion of contradiction for absolutes. It directly constructs the witness C.
This method (iftirad) is commonly used in Islamic logic for proving conversions.

Source Text

In summary, the correct view regarding the absolute proposition is that it converts as absolute: for if we say 'Every A is B', it follows that 'Some B is A'. The customary demonstration of this is: if not some B is A, then no B is A; this converts to no A is B, as previously shown; but it was given that every A is B; this is a contradiction. So this is the customary demonstration. Someone may object that it is not a genuine demonstration, for a genuine demonstration would require that the contradictory of the particular affirmative absolute is the universal negative absolute. There is discussion about this: the absolute has no contradictory of its own kind, but rather it is necessary to specify the state and time, which are not specified here, so it has no contradictory. But we say: this demonstration is correct even if the absolute has no contradictory of its own kind. If by 'genuine' is meant correct, then it is genuine; if something else is meant, that is suspended until clarified. That it is correct is because if someone says 'Some B is A' and it is false, it is false only because no B is A at any time; if it were found, it would be true. If it were true along with 'Every B is not A at some time', then the universal negative absolute does not contradict that particular affirmative. But when the particular affirmative is false, it is true, and that particular is false only if no part of the parts is described by the predicate at any time; therefore the contradictory of this particular absolute is this negative which was previously shown to convert as to its quality. By this it is clear that this demonstration is correct. As for one who takes absolute in a more specific sense, this is not its contradictory and can be false. Let its conversion be clarified by hypothesis: if every A is B, suppose one of the things described as B is A, let it be C; then C is A and B, so something described as B which is C is described as A, so some B is A. Otherwise, no B is A as an absolute negation, meaning as long as the essence of B is described as B; and it was given that every A is B, so it follows that no A is A; this is a contradiction.

Commentary
This passage is an extended treatment of the conversion rule for absolute (مطلقة) categorical propositions. The author begins by stating the rule: 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A'. He then presents the standard proof by contradiction: assume 'Some B is A' is false, i.e., 'No B is A'; this converts to 'No A is B', which contradicts the original 'Every A is B'. The author then addresses an objection: this proof is not genuine because absolute propositions have no true contradictory of their own type (since they lack temporal specification). The author defends the proof by arguing that if 'genuine' means logically correct, it is genuine. He further explains that the contradictory of a particular affirmative absolute is a universal negative absolute that holds precisely when the particular is false. The proof is thus valid. Finally, he provides an alternative proof by hypothesis: choose an individual C that is both A and B, directly showing that some B is A. This second proof is clearer and avoids the earlier subtlety.

Proof of Conversion of Universal Affirmative by Hypothesis
Author proves that every A is B implies some B is A using hypothetical reasoning and reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof of conversion of a universal affirmative proposition (كل أ ب) to a particular affirmative (بعض ب أ) using the method of hypothesis and reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Conversion of 'every A is B' to 'some B is A' is proven by assuming an arbitrary A and showing it must be B.

This passage demonstrates the foundational logical operation of conversion by hypothesis. While you might take conversion for granted, Islamic logicians rigorously proved it using immediate inference and reductio ad absurdum. Understanding this proof equips you to validate immediate inferences in theological and legal arguments where the validity of a converse may determine the soundness of an entire proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition to be converted
The original proposition is the universal affirmative absolute: 'Every A is B' (كل أ ب). The desired converse is the particular affirmative: 'Some B is A' (بعض ب أ).
2
Apply the method of hypothesis (iftirad)
Assume an arbitrary individual J that is A. Since every A is B, J must also be B. Thus, J is an example of a B that is A, so some B is A follows directly.
This step shows that if the original proposition holds, the converse cannot be false without contradiction.
3
Recognize the reductio ad absurdum (khalaf)
If we deny the converse and claim 'no B is A', then from 'every A is B' we deduce that every A is not A (since every A is B, and no B is A, so no A is A). This is a logical contradiction, proving the converse must be true.
The contradiction (no A is A) is absurd, validating the conversion.
4
Note the conditions for the proof
The proof works specifically for absolute (non-modal) propositions. The author clarifies the contradictory relationships to ensure the reductio is correctly set up.
In modal logic, conversion rules may differ; this proof applies to the unqualified absolute sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه إذا وجد يكون صادقاً. ولو كان صادقًا مع ذلك وهو أن كل «ب» ليس وقتا ما «أ» ، فليس إذن الكلية السالبة المطلقة تناقض تلك الموجبة الجزئية. ولكن إذا كذبت الجزئية الموجبة صدقت، ولا تكذب تلك الجزئية إلا وأن لا يكون بعض من الأبعاض موصوفًا في شيء من الأوقات بالمحمول، فنقيض هذه الجزئية المطلقة إذن هو هذه السالبة التي ظهر من حالها قبل أنها تنعكس كيفيتها؛ وبهذا يظهر أن هذا البيان حق. فإن أحد المطلق على المعنى الأخص لم يكن هذا نقيضه بل يمكن أن يكون كاذبًا فليتبين انعكاسه بالافتراض وذلك بأن يقال إذا كان كل «أب» فليفرض واحد من الموصوفات ب «أ» وليكن «ج»، فكون «ج» هو «أ» وهو «ب» فالموصوف بـ «ب» الذي هو «ج» موصوف بأنه «أ» فيكون بعض «ب أ». وإلا فلا شيء من «ب أ» سلبًا مطلقا يعني ما دام ذات «ب» موصوفة بأنها «ب»؛ وقد كان كل «أب» فينتج أن لا شيء من «أ أ»، هذا خلف.
For if it exists, it is true. And if it is true along with that, which is that every B is not at some time A, then the universal negative absolute does not contradict that particular affirmative. But when the particular affirmative is false, it is true, and that particular is only false when no part is described by the predicate at any time. So the contradictory of this particular absolute is this negative whose character appeared before to convert; and by this it appears that this statement is true. For one of the absolute in the specific sense, this was not its contradictory, but it can be false. So let its conversion be shown by hypothesis. That is by saying: If every A is B, then suppose one of the things described by A, and let it be J. Then J is A and it is B, so the thing described by B which is J is described by A, so some B is A. Otherwise, no B is A as an absolute negation, mean­ing as long as the essence of B is described as B; and it was that every A is B, so it results that no A is A, this is a contradiction.

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof demonstrating the conversion of a universal affirmative absolute proposition to a particular affirmative absolute proposition. The author begins by discussing the contradictory relationships between absolute propositions, clarifying that the universal negative absolute contradicts the particular affirmative absolute. He then introduces the concept of conversion by hypothesis (iftiraad) and provides a step-by-step proof: assume every A is B, pick an arbitrary individual J that is A; since J is A and every A is B, J is B; therefore some B is A. If we deny that some B is A (i.e., claim no B is A absolutely), then from every A is B we derive that no A is A, which is a logical contradiction. This proof uses the method of reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) and establishes the validity of the conversion rule for non-modal (mutlaq) propositions. The earlier sentences about contradictory relations set the stage by identifying the precise logical opposite of the particular affirmative, which is the universal negative that converts in quality (i.e., the contradictory of 'some B is A' is 'no B is A'). The author then shows that the conversion holds specifically for the absolute sense, not for other modal senses.

Conversion of Universal Necessary Affirmative in Real Propositions
Author proves conversion of necessary universal affirmative in real propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that a universal necessary affirmative in a real proposition converts to a particular necessary affirmative, and identify the logical steps involved.

Core Idea
In real propositions, 'every J is necessarily B' entails 'some B is necessarily J' — proven by contradiction using modal and conversion rules.

We have already learned the basic rules of conversion for categorical propositions. But how do these rules apply when modality is involved? The author introduces a distinction between external and real propositions, and then provides a proof for the conversion of necessary universal affirmatives in real propositions. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish external vs. real propositions
Identify the two types of actual affirmative propositions. External propositions consider actual existents; real propositions consider essences. Note that the conversion rule applies differently.
2
State the conversion claim
For real propositions, the universal necessary affirmative 'Every J is necessarily B' converts to a particular necessary affirmative 'Some B is necessarily J'. This is the claim to be proved.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory 'No B is J by general possibility' is true. But from the original 'Every J is necessarily B' we can derive 'Every J is B by general possibility' (since necessity entails possibility). Then by conversion of the absolute universal affirmative (which is valid), we get 'Some B is J by general possibility'. This contradicts the assumption. Therefore the converse must be true.
The proof uses two steps: modal drop from necessity to possibility, and conversion of the absolute universal affirmative.
4
Note the condition: real proposition
The proof works only if the proposition is real (haqiqiyya). In external propositions, the conversion rule does not yield a necessary conclusion uniformly. Understand why the essence-based consideration matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد زعم قوم من المتقدمين في الموجبات الفعلية «أنها إذا كانت خارجية فلا تختلف بين الكلية منها والجزئية في الأحكام التي مرَّ ذكرها. فأما إذا كانت حقيقية فالموجبة الكلية تنعكس في جميعها جزئية ضرورية حتى إذا صدق قولنا كل ج ب بالضرورة صدق قولنا بعض ب ج بالضرورة، وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا لا شيء من ب ج بالإمكان العام»؛ وذلك محال لما صدق قولنا كل ج ب بالضرورة. فإنه إذا صدق قولنا كل ج ب بالضرورة صدق قولنا كل ج ب بالإمكان العام فيصدق قولنا بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام مع أنه لا يصدق أصلا؛ وذلك محال. ولا يقال إنه لا يكون عكس ذلك فإن قولنا بعض ب ج بالضرورة لازم الثبوت في نفس الأمر إذا كانت القضية حقيقية ضرورة أن الموصوف بهما بالضرورة بحيث إذا دخل في الوجود كان ب لا محالة وهو بحيث إذا دخل في الوجود كان ج بالضرورة؛ فإذن هذه القضية لازمة الصدق بتقدير صدق الأصل وكذبه . ولو كان كذلك فلا يكون عكس تلك القضية التي هي الأصل؛ لأنا لا نعني بالعكس إلا ملازمة العكس للأصل. ولا يمتنع أن يكون اللازم في المتّصلة الصادقة واجب الصدق دون ما يستلزمه لما أنه أعم، وكان صادقًا على تقدير صدقه وكذبه، وهذا ما قاله قوم من المتقدمين؛ وفيه من الكلام فإنه من جملة ما يتوقف صدقه على تحرير المبحث وهو أن المراد بالضرورة في قولنا كل ج ب بالضرورة ما هو . فإن من الممكن أن يكون كل ج من حيث هو ج بالضرورة ب ولا يلزم أن يكون بعض ب من حيث هو ب بالضرورة ج. ولا يقال المراد من الضرورة فيما نحن فيه هو الضرورة بحسب الذات؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العكس ضروريًا، فإنه لا يلزم من أن يكون هذا ذلك بحسب الذات أن يكون ذلك هذا بحسب الذات كذلك؛ وفيه من الكلام كما في ذلك.
And a group of the predecessors claimed regarding actual affirmative propositions that if they are external, then there is no difference between universal and particular in the rulings that have been mentioned. But if they are real, then the universal affirmative converts in all its cases to a particular necessary, such that if our statement 'every J is necessarily B' is true, then our statement 'some B is necessarily J' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'no B is J by general possibility'; and that is impossible because our statement 'every J is necessarily B' is true. For if our statement 'every J is necessarily B' is true, then our statement 'every J is B by general possibility' is true, so our statement 'some B is J by general possibility' is true, while it is not true at all; that is impossible. And it is not said that the converse of that is not the case, for our statement 'some B is necessarily J' is necessarily true in itself if the proposition is real, because the thing described by both is necessarily such that if it enters existence, it is B necessarily, and it is such that if it enters existence, it is J necessarily; so this proposition is necessarily true given the truth of the original and its falsity. And if it were so, then it would not be the converse of that original proposition; for we do not mean by converse except the entailment of the converse to the original. And it is not impossible that the necessary in a true conditional is necessarily true without what entails it, because it is more general, and it is true on the supposition of its truth and falsity. And this is what a group of the predecessors said. There is discussion in it, for it is among the things whose truth depends on re-examination of the topic, namely what is meant by 'necessity' in our statement 'every J is necessarily B'. For it is possible that every J from the perspective that it is J is necessarily B, but it does not follow that some B from the perspective that it is B is necessarily J. And it is not said that the intended meaning of necessity here is necessity by essence; then it would follow that the converse is necessary, for it does not follow from this being that by essence that that is this by essence likewise. There is discussion in it as in that.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage addresses the conversion of modal propositions, specifically the universal necessary affirmative in real (haqiqi) propositions. The author first distinguishes between external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) propositions. For external propositions, the rules of conversion do not differentiate between universals and particulars as previously discussed. For real propositions, the universal necessary affirmative 'every J is necessarily B' is claimed to convert to a particular necessary affirmative 'some B is necessarily J'. The argument: if the converse were false, then its contradictory 'no B is J by general possibility' would be true; but from 'every J is necessarily B', we can derive 'every J is B by general possibility' (since necessity entails possibility), and then 'some B is J by general possibility', which contradicts the assumption that 'no B is J by general possibility' is true. Therefore, the converse must be true. However, an objection is raised: the converse 'some B is necessarily J' might be necessarily true in itself independently of the original, which would mean it is not a genuine converse (since conversion requires dependency). The author notes that a necessary consequent in a true conditional can be true even when the antecedent is false, so it is not impossible. The passage then criticizes this view, pointing out that the meaning of 'necessity' must be clarified: it is possible that J is necessarily B qua J, but B is not necessarily J qua B. Even if we interpret necessity as 'by essence' (bi-hasab al-dhat), it does not follow that the relation is symmetric. The author leaves the discussion open, noting that further analysis is needed. This passage exemplifies the depth of modal logic in the Islamic tradition and the critical examination of conversion rules.

حقيقية
الضرورة

In real propositions, the universal necessary affirmative converts to a particular necessary affirmative via a proof by contradiction that uses modal entailment and conversion of absolute universal affirmative.

The Debate on Necessity: Symmetry and Essence
Author discusses objection to conversion and refines the notion of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessity per se and necessity per aliud in conversion, and evaluate the objection that the converse might be true independently.

Core Idea
Necessity is not automatically symmetric: 'J is necessarily B by essence' does not imply 'B is necessarily J by essence'.

The previous lesson established the conversion rule for necessary universal affirmatives in real propositions. However, a deeper question arises: does the converse inherit the same kind of necessity? The author presents an objection from earlier logicians and then offers a critical analysis, clarifying that necessity must be understood in terms of essence (bi-ḥasab al-dhāt). This lesson unpacks the objection and the author's refinement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Some predecessors argued that the converse 'some B is necessarily J' might be necessarily true in itself, independent of the original. If so, it would not be a genuine 'converse', because conversion requires that the truth of the converse depends on the truth of the original.
The objection hinges on the notion of logical dependency (luzūm).
2
Examine the reply
The author notes that a necessary consequent in a true conditional can be true even if the antecedent is false. So it is not impossible for the converse to be necessarily true independently. However, this does not settle the issue.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author points out that we must clarify what 'necessity' means in 'every J is necessarily B'. It could be that J is necessarily B from the perspective of its essence (bi-ḥasab al-dhāt). But even then, it does not follow that some B is necessarily J from the perspective of its essence. Why? Because the essence of J and the essence of B are different; the relation is not symmetric.
For example, 'Every human is necessarily rational' but 'Every rational being is not necessarily human' (there could be other rational beings like angels in Islamic thought).
4
Apply the distinction
Test the conversion with a non-symmetric essential relation. This shows the limitation of the conversion rule: it only holds in a weaker sense (the particular necessary affirmative is true, but the necessity is not intrinsic to the subject of the converse).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد زعم قوم من المتقدمين في الموجبات الفعلية «أنها إذا كانت خارجية فلا تختلف بين الكلية منها والجزئية في الأحكام التي مرَّ ذكرها. فأما إذا كانت حقيقية فالموجبة الكلية تنعكس في جميعها جزئية ضرورية حتى إذا صدق قولنا كل ج ب بالضرورة صدق قولنا بعض ب ج بالضرورة، وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا لا شيء من ب ج بالإمكان العام»؛ وذلك محال لما صدق قولنا كل ج ب بالضرورة. فإنه إذا صدق قولنا كل ج ب بالضرورة صدق قولنا كل ج ب بالإمكان العام فيصدق قولنا بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام مع أنه لا يصدق أصلا؛ وذلك محال. ولا يقال إنه لا يكون عكس ذلك فإن قولنا بعض ب ج بالضرورة لازم الثبوت في نفس الأمر إذا كانت القضية حقيقية ضرورة أن الموصوف بهما بالضرورة بحيث إذا دخل في الوجود كان ب لا محالة وهو بحيث إذا دخل في الوجود كان ج بالضرورة؛ فإذن هذه القضية لازمة الصدق بتقدير صدق الأصل وكذبه . ولو كان كذلك فلا يكون عكس تلك القضية التي هي الأصل؛ لأنا لا نعني بالعكس إلا ملازمة العكس للأصل. ولا يمتنع أن يكون اللازم في المتّصلة الصادقة واجب الصدق دون ما يستلزمه لما أنه أعم، وكان صادقًا على تقدير صدقه وكذبه، وهذا ما قاله قوم من المتقدمين؛ وفيه من الكلام فإنه من جملة ما يتوقف صدقه على تحرير المبحث وهو أن المراد بالضرورة في قولنا كل ج ب بالضرورة ما هو . فإن من الممكن أن يكون كل ج من حيث هو ج بالضرورة ب ولا يلزم أن يكون بعض ب من حيث هو ب بالضرورة ج. ولا يقال المراد من الضرورة فيما نحن فيه هو الضرورة بحسب الذات؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العكس ضروريًا، فإنه لا يلزم من أن يكون هذا ذلك بحسب الذات أن يكون ذلك هذا بحسب الذات كذلك؛ وفيه من الكلام كما في ذلك.
And a group of the predecessors claimed regarding actual affirmative propositions that if they are external, then there is no difference between universal and particular in the rulings that have been mentioned. But if they are real, then the universal affirmative converts in all its cases to a particular necessary, such that if our statement 'every J is necessarily B' is true, then our statement 'some B is necessarily J' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'no B is J by general possibility'; and that is impossible because our statement 'every J is necessarily B' is true. For if our statement 'every J is necessarily B' is true, then our statement 'every J is B by general possibility' is true, so our statement 'some B is J by general possibility' is true, while it is not true at all; that is impossible. And it is not said that the converse of that is not the case, for our statement 'some B is necessarily J' is necessarily true in itself if the proposition is real, because the thing described by both is necessarily such that if it enters existence, it is B necessarily, and it is such that if it enters existence, it is J necessarily; so this proposition is necessarily true given the truth of the original and its falsity. And if it were so, then it would not be the converse of that original proposition; for we do not mean by converse except the entailment of the converse to the original. And it is not impossible that the necessary in a true conditional is necessarily true without what entails it, because it is more general, and it is true on the supposition of its truth and falsity. And this is what a group of the predecessors said. There is discussion in it, for it is among the things whose truth depends on re-examination of the topic, namely what is meant by 'necessity' in our statement 'every J is necessarily B'. For it is possible that every J from the perspective that it is J is necessarily B, but it does not follow that some B from the perspective that it is B is necessarily J. And it is not said that the intended meaning of necessity here is necessity by essence; then it would follow that the converse is necessary, for it does not follow from this being that by essence that that is this by essence likewise. There is discussion in it as in that.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage addresses the conversion of modal propositions, specifically the universal necessary affirmative in real (haqiqi) propositions. The author first distinguishes between external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) propositions. For external propositions, the rules of conversion do not differentiate between universals and particulars as previously discussed. For real propositions, the universal necessary affirmative 'every J is necessarily B' is claimed to convert to a particular necessary affirmative 'some B is necessarily J'. The argument: if the converse were false, then its contradictory 'no B is J by general possibility' would be true; but from 'every J is necessarily B', we can derive 'every J is B by general possibility' (since necessity entails possibility), and then 'some B is J by general possibility', which contradicts the assumption that 'no B is J by general possibility' is true. Therefore, the converse must be true. However, an objection is raised: the converse 'some B is necessarily J' might be necessarily true in itself independently of the original, which would mean it is not a genuine converse (since conversion requires dependency). The author notes that a necessary consequent in a true conditional can be true even when the antecedent is false, so it is not impossible. The passage then criticizes this view, pointing out that the meaning of 'necessity' must be clarified: it is possible that J is necessarily B qua J, but B is not necessarily J qua B. Even if we interpret necessity as 'by essence' (bi-hasab al-dhat), it does not follow that the relation is symmetric. The author leaves the discussion open, noting that further analysis is needed. This passage exemplifies the depth of modal logic in the Islamic tradition and the critical examination of conversion rules.

الإمكان العام

Critique of Modal Conversion Interdependence
Author critiques different views on conversion of necessary and possible propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate different positions on modal conversion and identify the author's critique of claims that necessary and possible conversions are interdependent.

Core Idea
Modal conversion rules are not all logically necessary; they require proof, not mere interdependence.

Conversion (in‘ikās) is a fundamental immediate inference in logic. This passage shows a medieval logician critically analysing various views on how necessity, possibility, and assertoric quality affect conversion. He objects to a group's claim that the truth of a possible conversion and a necessary conversion are interdependent, arguing that this does not follow logically. We will dissect his objections and understand the methodological lesson: truth requires proof (burhān) and rejection of blind imitation (taqlīd).

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions on conversion of necessity/assertoric
The author lists three views: some say necessity and assertoric converts to possible-general; some say to general-absolute; some say in the true universal it remains necessary, unlike in external propositions. Note the diversity.
2
Analyse the argument against converting external possibles
A group claims that no external possible proposition converts. Their reasoning: two different types could both have a property by special possibility, but only one actually has it; the converse would not hold universally. The author objects that if the original is true, the converse can be true—this is in the realm of denial (fī ḥayyiz al-man‘).
The author refers to a previous argument (limā marra min qabl).
3
Examine the interdependence claim and its refutation
The same group claims that the truth of converting a possible affirmative and a necessary affirmative are interdependent because the proof of one relies on the other. The author points out that this interdependence does not follow from their reasoning—it is also in the realm of denial. Only if one insists that the proof of either requires the other would interdependence be necessary.
The author uses the phrase 'fī ḥayyiz al-man‘' to indicate the claim is not accepted without further proof.
4
Distinguish external vs. essential possibles
The author notes that the above discussion applies when possibles are external (khārijiyya). If they are essential (ḥaqīqiyya), the conversion is suspended (tawaqquf) according to this group, similar to the case of necessary negative conversion (to be discussed later).
This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of the controversy.
5
Extract the methodological conclusion
The author concludes that the closest view to reason is that of the Master (Ibn Sīnā). Truth is only accessible through proof (burhān), which requires turning away from taqlīd (imitation) and critically examining opinions.
This is a reminder of the broader epistemological stance of the author.

وبالجملة فالضرورة من الفعليات بل الضرورة وغيرها من الموجبات تنعكس ممكنة عامة عند قوم كما مرَّ، ومطلقة عامة عند قوم كذلك. وعند قوم إنها في الكلية الحقيقية ضرورية بخلاف ما يكون في الخارجية. وأما الممكنات فعند هذا القوم لا ينعكس شيء منها إذا كانت خارجية، فإنه يمكن أن يكون كل واحد من النوعين المتباينين يكون كذا بالإمكان الخاص؛ وذلك يكون لأحدهما بالفعل دون الثاني فيصدق أن كل ما له النوع الثاني بالفعل فله ذلك الأمر بالإمكان الخاص ولا يصدق في عكسه بعض ما له ذلك الأمر بالفعل فله النوع الثاني ضرورة انحصار ما له ذلك الأمر بالفعل في أفراد النوع الأول. غير أنه في حيز المنع فإنه يصدق إذا كان الأصل صادقًا لما مرَّ من قبل. وأيضًا إن هذا القوم زعموا أن الصدق في انعكاس الموجبة الممكنة مع الصدق في انعكاس الضرورية أمران متلازمان لما أن البرهان على هذا يتم بذلك، وعلى ذلك بهذا كذلك. ولو كان كذلك لكان ما يصدق به أحدهما يصدق الآخر، وما يقدح في أحدهما يقدح في الآخر. وهذا أيضًا في حيز المنع لما أنه لا يلزم من هذا التركيب أنهما أمران متلازمان حتى يلزم أنه كذا وكذا. اللهم إلا أن يقال إنه لا يصدق بالبرهان إلا بذلك؛ وذلك لا يصدق أيضًا إلا بهذا فإنه إذا كان كذلك كان من اللوازم أن يكونا متلازمين، هذا إذا كانت الممكنات خارجية. فأما إذا كانت حقيقية فاللازم فيها التوقف عندهم كما في عكس السالبة الضرورية، والكلام في السالبة الضرورية يجيء من بعد. وبالجملة فالأقوال المختلفة في الانعكاس متعددة؛ وما هو الأقرب منها إلى العقل فذلك ما ذهب إليه الشيخ. والحقُّ لا يظهر إلا بالبرهان، والبرهان لا يظهر إلا بالإعراض عن التقليد، والإعراض على الأقوال بقدر الإمكان، والله المستعان وعليه التكلان.
In summary, necessity from the actuals, rather necessity and others from the assertoric [propositions] convert to possible-general according to some people as has passed, and to general-absolute according to some people likewise. And according to some people, it is in the true universal necessary contrary to what is in the external. As for the possible [propositions], according to these people none of them convert if they are external, for it is possible that every one of the two different types is such by special possibility; and that is for one of them actually without the second, so it is true that everything that has the second type actually has that matter by special possibility, and in its converse it is not true that some of what has that matter actually has the second type due to the necessary confinement of what has that matter actually to individuals of the first type. Except that it is in the realm of denial, for it is true if the original is true for what has passed before. Also, these people claimed that the truth in the conversion of the possible affirmative with the truth in the conversion of the necessary are two interdependent things because the proof for this is completed by that, and for that by this likewise. And if it were so, what makes one true would make the other true, and what impugns one would impugn the other. This too is in the realm of denial because it does not follow from this composition that they are interdependent such that such-and-such follows. Unless it is said that it is not true by proof except by that; and that is not true except by this; for if it is so, it would be necessary that they are interdependent. This is if the possibles are external. But if they are true [essential], then the required [conversion] is suspended according to them as in the conversion of the necessary negative, and the discussion on the necessary negative will come later. In summary, the different opinions on conversion are numerous; and what is closest among them to reason is what the Master (al-Shaykh) held. Truth does not appear except by proof, and proof does not appear except by turning away from imitation, and turning away from opinions as much as possible. And God is the Aid and upon Him is reliance.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of various opinions on the conversion of modal propositions, specifically necessary and possible propositions. The author begins by summarizing the state of the debate: some hold that necessity and other assertoric propositions convert to possible-general (i.e., the converse is a possible proposition in the general sense), while others maintain that in the true universal (al-kulliyya al-haqīqiyya) the converse is necessary, unlike in external propositions. Moving to possibles, a certain group (qawm) claims that no possible proposition converts if it is external (khārijiyya). Their reasoning involves a scenario where two different types (naw‘ayn mutabāyinayn) have the same attribute by special possibility, but only one actually possesses it; the converse would not hold universally because the attribute is confined to the first type. The author then objects (fī ḥayyiz al-man‘) that the converse can be true if the original proposition is true, as previously established. Next, the author addresses another claim by this group: that the truth of the conversion of a possible affirmative and the truth of the conversion of a necessary affirmative are interdependent (mutalāzimān), since the proof of one relies on the other. The author counters that this interdependence does not necessarily follow from their reasoning—it is also in the realm of denial. Only if one insists that the proof of one requires the other would interdependence be necessary. The author then distinguishes between external possibles (where the above applies) and true (essential) possibles (ḥaqīqiyya), where the conversion is suspended (tawaqquf) according to this group, similar to the case of the necessary negative, which will be discussed later. In conclusion, the author notes the multiplicity of opinions and declares that the closest to reason is the view of the Master (Ibn Sīnā). True knowledge requires proof (burhān) and turning away from blind imitation (taqlīd). This passage demonstrates the author's method of critically examining logical doctrines and insisting on rational demonstration.

Conversion of Necessary Universal Negatives and the Circularity Objection
Author defines conditions for conversion of negative propositions and proves necessary negative converts to itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reproduce the proof that a necessary universal negative proposition converts to itself as a necessary universal negative, and identify the circularity objection and the author's response strategy.

Core Idea
A necessary universal negative converts to itself, but the proof must avoid circular dependence on other conversions.

In this passage, the author lays out the conditions for the conversion of negative categorical propositions, focusing on necessary negatives. He provides a proof by contradiction and then considers an objection that his proof leads to circularity. Understanding this demonstrates how modal logic handles conversion without falling into petitio principii.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The author states that only universal negatives can convert, and among them, only those with necessity, permanence, or certain conditional modalities convert as themselves. Note the distinction between conversion 'as itself' and other forms.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume the necessary universal negative 'Necessarily, no C is B' is true. If its converse 'Necessarily, no B is C' were false, then its contradictory 'Some B is C by general possibility' would be true. This particular affirmative converts to 'Some C is B by general possibility', contradicting the original necessary universal negative.
The proof relies on the principle that if a necessary universal negative is true, its contradictory (particular affirmative with possibility) cannot hold.
3
Analyze the circularity objection
An objector claims that the proof for the universal negative's conversion depends on the conversion of the particular affirmative, and vice versa, creating a vicious circle (dawr).
The objector references an earlier relation between these conversions.
4
Examine the author's response
The author argues that circularity only arises if there is a single path (ṭarīq wāḥid) to establish both conversions. If multiple independent paths exist, no circularity follows. The response is cut off, but the core idea is that the proof can be grounded independently.
This defense preserves the validity of the conversion rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في السوالب من الحمليات ]
وأما في السوالب فإنها لا تكون منعكسة إلا وأن تكون كلية، والكلية منها لا تكون منعكسة كنفسها إلا وأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة أو مشروطة عامة أو عرفية عامة. فإن ما عداها فذلك لا ينعكس أصلا عند الجمهور إلا المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة لكن العكس فيهما لا يكون كنفسهما؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
أما الضرورية فإنها تنعكس ضرورية فإن من المحال أن يتخيل حصول الشيء مع غيره دائمًا ولا يُتخيل حصول الغير معه. فلو صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من (ج) ب) صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من (ب) ج وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام. وإنه ينعكس عكسا جزئياً وهو قولنا بعض (ج) ب) بالإمكان العام، وقد كان لا شيء من (ج) ب) بالضرورة، هذا خلف.
ثم لقائل أن يقول : لو كان كما ذكرتم لكانت السالبة الكلية لا تنعكس سالبة كلية إلا وأن تكون الموجبة الجزئية تنعكس موجبة جزئية. والموجبة الجزئية لا تنعكس موجبة جزئية إلا وأن تكون السالبة الكلية تنعكس سالبة كلية كما مرَّ من قبل. فالصحة في السالبة الكلية مفتقرة إلى الصحة في الموجبة الجزئية، والصحة في الموجبة الجزئية مفتقرة إلى الصحة في السالبة الكلية : فيلزم الدور؛ وذلك محال لاستحالة افتقار صحة الشيء إلى صحة ذلك الشيء. لكنا نقول إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان الصحة طريق واحد في السالبة الكلية وفي الموجبة الجزئية. فأما إذا كان الطريق متعددا فلا؛ فإنه يمكن أن يحصل الصحة في السالبة الكلية بطريق، وفي الموجبة بطريق لا يفتقر إلى ذلك، ولا
Section: [On Negative Propositions from the Categoricals]
As for the negatives, they are not converted unless they are universal, and the universal of them is not converted as itself unless it is necessary or permanent or generally conditional or generally customary. For what is other than these, it does not convert at all according to the majority except the specially conditional and specially customary, but the conversion in them is not as themselves; that will be known later.
As for the necessary [negative], it converts as necessary, for it is impossible to imagine the occurrence of a thing with another always without imagining the occurrence of the other with it. So if our statement 'Necessarily, no C is B' is true, then our statement 'Necessarily, no B is C' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some B is C by general possibility.' And it converts partially, which is our statement 'Some C is B by general possibility,' but it was [originally] 'Necessarily, no C is B,' which is a contradiction.
Then an objector may say: If it were as you mentioned, then the universal negative would not convert as a universal negative unless the particular affirmative converts as a particular affirmative. And the particular affirmative does not convert as a particular affirmative unless the universal negative converts as a universal negative, as has passed earlier. So the truth of the universal negative depends on the truth of the particular affirmative, and the truth of the particular affirmative depends on the truth of the universal negative: thus circularity (dawr) follows, which is impossible because it is impossible for the truth of a thing to depend on the truth of that same thing. But we say: That would be the case only if the truth [of the conversion] has a single path in the universal negative and in the particular affirmative. But if the path is multiple, then not; for it is possible that the truth in the universal negative is obtained by one path, and in the affirmative by a path that does not depend on that, and not
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the conversion of negative categorical propositions. The author first states a general rule: only universal negatives can be converted, and among them, only those that are necessary, permanent, generally conditional, or generally customary convert as themselves (i.e., the converse has the same modality). Other types either do not convert at all, or convert to a different form (like specially conditional or specially customary).
The author then provides a proof for the conversion of necessary universal negatives. The proof is by contradiction: if 'Necessarily, no C is B' is true, then its converse 'Necessarily, no B is C' must also be true. If not, the contradictory ('Some B is C by general possibility') would be true, which then converts to 'Some C is B by general possibility,' contradicting the original necessary universal negative. This shows that the converse must be necessary universal negative.
Next, an objection is raised: this reasoning seems to lead to circularity (dawr). The objector argues that the conversion of the universal negative depends on the conversion of the particular affirmative, and vice versa, based on earlier relations between these conversions. If the truth of one depends on the truth of the other in a mutual way, that is impossible. The author's response begins: this circularity would only occur if there is a single path (ṭarīq wāḥid) to establish the truth of both conversions. But if there are multiple independent paths, then no circularity arises. The response is cut off, but the implication is that the proof for the necessary negative conversion can stand on its own without relying on the particular affirmative conversion. This defense is a standard logical move to avoid the charge of circular reasoning.

ضرورية (موجبة/سالبة)
عكس

C) By citing Aristotle's authority on conversion.

Key Takeaway
The necessary universal negative proposition converts to itself necessarily, and the proof avoids circularity by not relying on a single dependent path.
