Four Modal Types That Convert as Themselves
Author classifies which negative universal propositions convert as themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modal types of universal negative propositions convert as themselves and which do not, according to the classification in this passage.

Core Idea
Only four modal types of universal negatives convert as themselves: necessary, permanent, generally conditioned, generally customary.

In this section, the author narrows down which negative universal propositions can undergo conversion and preserve their modal type. Not every universal negative converts as itself; only those carrying specific modal qualifiers. This classification is essential for correctly applying immediate inference in categorical logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition for conversion
Remember that only universal negative propositions can be converted. The author says 'لا تكون منعكسة إلا وأن تكون كلية' – they are not converted unless they are universal.
This is a well-known rule: only universal negatives (E propositions) convert to universal negatives.
2
Identify the four modal types that convert as themselves
From the list: ضرورية (necessary), دائمة (permanent), مشروطة عامة (generally conditioned), عرفية عامة (generally customary). These four convert to a universal negative of the same modal type.
These are the only ones the author mentions for conversion 'كنفسها' (as themselves).
3
Note the exceptions: specially conditioned and specially customary
The author says other types do not convert at all, except the specially conditioned (مشروطة خاصة) and specially customary (عرفية خاصة), but their conversion is not 'like themselves' – meaning the result differs in modality.
The details of these exceptions are deferred to later sections.
4
Apply the classification to a concrete example
Take a necessary universal negative: 'No human is a stone necessarily.' Its converse is also necessary: 'No stone is a human necessarily.' But a possible universal negative (e.g., 'No human is a writer possibly') does not convert as itself; its conversion would not be a possible universal negative.
This illustrates why the modal qualifier matters for immediate inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في السوالب من الحمليات ] وأما في السوالب فإنها لا تكون منعكسة إلا وأن تكون كلية، والكلية منها لا تكون منعكسة كنفسها إلا وأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة أو مشروطة عامة أو عرفية عامة. فإن ما عداها فذلك لا ينعكس أصلا عند الجمهور إلا المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة لكن العكس فيهما لا يكون كنفسهما؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
Section: On negative categorical propositions. As for negative propositions, they are not converted unless they are universal. And the universal among them is not converted as itself unless it is necessary, or permanent, or generally conditioned, or generally customary. For other than these, they do not convert at all according to the majority, except the specially conditioned and the specially customary, but their conversion is not like themselves; and that is known later.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is a classification. It states that only universal negative propositions can be converted (عكس). Among those, only four modal types convert to a universal negative of the same modal type: necessary (ضرورية), permanent (دائمة), generally conditioned (مشروطة عامة), and generally customary (عرفية عامة). All other types do not convert at all, except for two special types (specially conditioned and specially customary) which do convert, but not to a universal negative of the same type—they convert to a different form. The passage ends by noting that the details of those exceptions will be explained later. This classification is foundational for understanding immediate inferences in categorical logic.

Only universal negative propositions with necessity, permanence, general conditionality, or general custom convert as themselves; all others either do not convert or convert with a change in modal type.

Proof That Necessary Universal Negative Converts Necessarily
Author proves conversion of necessary negative universals and refutes circularity objection with two methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that a necessary universal negative proposition (e.g., 'Necessarily, no J is B') converts to a necessary universal negative using a contradiction argument.

Core Idea
A necessary negative universal converts necessarily: if necessarily no J is B, then necessarily no B is J.

This lesson walks through the author's direct proof that conversion preserves the necessity modality. The proof uses the fact that if the conversion were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to a contradiction with the original proposition.

Guided Path
1
State the original proposition
Start with the given necessary universal negative: 'Necessarily, no J is B' (بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب). This is the premise that we assume true.
The proposition is a universal negative with the modality of necessity (ضرورية).
2
Assume the conversion is false
Suppose the converted proposition 'Necessarily, no B is J' is false. Then its contradictory is true: 'Some B is J with general possibility' (بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام). This is a particular affirmative with general possibility.
The contradictory of a universal negative (E) is a particular affirmative (I). In modal logic, the modality shifts from necessity to possibility.
3
Convert the particular affirmative
Convert the proposition 'Some B is J with general possibility' by swapping subject and predicate. Since conversion of a particular affirmative yields a particular affirmative with the same modality, we obtain 'Some J is B with general possibility'.
This conversion is valid because particular affirmatives convert to particular affirmatives, regardless of modality (as long as possibility is preserved).
4
Derive a contradiction
Now we have both 'Necessarily, no J is B' (original) and 'Some J is B with general possibility' (from conversion). These are contradictories: one says none, the other says some. This is impossible.
The contradiction shows that our assumption (the conversion is false) must be false; hence the conversion is true.
5
Conclude the conversion holds
Since assuming the conversion false leads to a contradiction, the conversion must be true. Therefore, the necessary universal negative converts to a necessary universal negative.
The proof relies on the validity of conversion for particular affirmatives and the logical relation of contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الضرورية فإنها تنعكس ضرورية فإن من المحال أن يتخيل حصول الشيء مع غيره دائمًا ولا يُتخيل حصول الغير معه. فلو صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من (ج) ب) الصدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من بج» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام. وإنه ينعكس عكسا جزئياً وهو قولنا بعض (ج) ب» بالإمكان العام، وقد كان لا شيء من (ج) ب) [۱۳۱] بالضرورة، هذا خلف. ثم لقائل أن يقول : لو كان كما ذكرتم لكانت السالبة الكلية لا تنعكس سالبة كلية إلا وأن تكون الموجبة الجزئية تنعكس موجبة جزئية. والموجبة الجزئية لا تنعكس موجبة جزئية إلا وأن تكون السالبة الكلية تنعكس سالبة كلية كما مرَّ من قبل. فالصحة في السالبة الكلية مفتقرة إلى الصحة في الموجبة الجزئية، والصحة في الموجبة الجزئية مفتقرة إلى الصحة في السالبة الكلية : فيلزم الدور؛ وذلك محال لاستحالة افتقار صحة الشيء إلى صحة ذلك الشيء. لكنا نقول إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان الصحة طريق واحد في السالبة الكلية وفي الموجبة الجزئية. فأما إذا كان الطريق متعددا فلا؛ فإنه يمكن أن يحصل الصحة في السالبة الكلية بطريق، وفي الموجبة بطريق لا يفتقر إلى ذلك، ولا ذلك إلى هذا. ألا ترى أنه يمكن بيان كل واحدة منهما بطريق الانضمام تارةً، وبطريق الافتراض أخرى. [1] أما طريق الانضمام فذلك مثل ما يقال إنه إذا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام يضم هذا إلى الأصل فيقال بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام، ولا شيء من ج ب بالضرورة؛ فبعض ب ليس (ب) بالضرورة، هذا خلف. [ب] وأما طريق الافتراض فإن يقال إذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من ج ب بالضرورة صدق قولنا لا شيء من ب ج ) بالضرورة وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض اب ج» بالإمكان العام فنفرضه أن يكون بالفعل فيكون بعض ب ج بالفعل، فذلك البعض بعينه «ب» و «ج» فذلك الجيم «ب» وذلك الباء (ج) لا محالة. وقد قلنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب»، هذا خلف. فلا يستراب في أن فرض الممكن موجودًا غير ممتنع وإلا لا يكون ممكناً؛ والكلام في كونه ممكنا. وعن البعض منهم في الجواب عن الشبهة المذكورة أن انعكاس الممكن ممكناً بين بنفسه. فإنه إذا أمكن أن يكون هذا الشيء ذلك أمكن أن يكون ذلك الشيء هذا. ولما كان هذا بينا بنفسه جاز تعريف غيره به من غير أن يفتقر تعريفه إلى تعريف ذلك الغير غير أنه لا يصح إلا وأن يكون بينا بنفسه؛ وفيه من الكلام
As for the necessary, it converts as necessary. For it is impossible to conceive that a thing constantly occurs with another while the other does not occur with it. So if our statement 'Necessarily, no J is B' is true, then our statement 'Necessarily, no B is J' is true; otherwise, its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some B is J with general possibility'. And it converts partially as our statement 'Some J is B with general possibility', but it was [the case that] 'Necessarily, no J is B' — this is a contradiction. Then an objector may say: If it were as you mentioned, then the universal negative would not convert as universal negative unless the particular affirmative converts as particular affirmative, and the particular affirmative would not convert as particular affirmative unless the universal negative converts as universal negative, as previously. So the validity of the universal negative depends on the validity of the particular affirmative, and the validity of the particular affirmative depends on the validity of the universal negative: this entails circularity; and that is impossible because it is impossible that the validity of a thing depends on the validity of that same thing. But we say that it would be so only if the validity of the universal negative and the particular affirmative were through a single path. However, if the paths are multiple, then not; for it is possible that the validity of the universal negative is obtained through one path, and the validity of the particular affirmative through another path that does not depend on that, nor that on this. Do you not see that each of them can be demonstrated sometimes by the method of combination (al-indimam) and sometimes by the method of hypothesis (al-iftirad). [1] As for the method of combination, it is like saying: if its contradictory, i.e., our statement 'Some B is J with general possibility' is true, this is combined with the original, and it is said: 'Some B is J with general possibility, and no J is B necessarily'; then some B is not B necessarily — this is a contradiction. [B] As for the method of hypothesis, it is said: if our statement 'No J is B necessarily' is true, then our statement 'No B is J necessarily' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, i.e., our statement 'Some B is J with general possibility'. Then we hypothesize it to be actual, so some B is J actually. That particular B and J: that J is B and that B is J necessarily. But we said 'Necessarily, no J is B' — this is a contradiction. So there is no doubt that hypothesizing the possible as actual is not impossible, otherwise it would not be possible; and the discussion is about it being possible. And from some of them, in answering the mentioned doubt, that the conversion of the possible as possible is self-evident: for if it is possible that this thing is that, then it is possible that that thing is this. And since this is self-evident, it is permissible to define other things by it without its definition needing the definition of that other, except that it is only valid if it is self-evident; and there is further discussion about this.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a refutation of an objection. It first proves that a necessary universal negative proposition (e.g., 'Necessarily, no J is B') converts to a necessary universal negative. The proof is by contradiction: if the converted version were false, its contradictory (a particular affirmative with general possibility) would be true, but that particular affirmative converts back to a particular affirmative which contradicts the original universal negative. Then an objection is raised: the proof seems to rely on the conversion of the particular affirmative, which itself depends on the conversion of the universal negative, creating a circular dependency. The author responds that the circularity only occurs if there is a single method of proof. But in fact, two independent methods exist: the method of combination (انضمام) and the method of hypothesis (افتراض). The method of combination uses the original universal negative together with the contradictory of the converted version to derive a contradiction. The method of hypothesis assumes the converted version is false, instantiates the possible particular affirmative as actual, and then derives a contradiction with the original universal negative. The passage also mentions an alternative response: the conversion of a possible proposition is self-evident and does not require proof, thus breaking the circle. This clarifies that the conversion of necessary negatives is demonstrable without circularity.

Resolving the Circularity Objection: Two Independent Proof Methods
Author proves conversion of necessary negative universals and refutes circularity objection with two methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the circularity objection to the conversion proof is refuted by presenting two independent methods: combination (indimam) and hypothesis (iftirad).

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided when multiple independent methods exist; combination and hypothesis break the circle.

An objector argues that the conversion proof is circular because it depends on the conversion of the particular affirmative, which itself depends on the universal negative. The author responds by showing that there are multiple independent methods to prove each conversion, making circularity impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims: The proof of universal negative conversion uses the conversion of the particular affirmative. But the conversion of the particular affirmative itself relies on the conversion of the universal negative (as previously established). This creates a circular dependency: each depends on the other.
This kind of circularity (dawr) is a logical fallacy because it makes the proof invalid.
2
Identify the key assumption
The author points out that the objection only holds if there is a single method of proof for each conversion. If there are multiple independent methods, then the circularity disappears because the proofs do not depend on each other.
The assumption of a single path is the weak point of the objection.
3
Describe the combination method (indimam)
In the combination method, we assume the contradictory of the converted universal negative (i.e., 'Some B is J with possibility') is true. Combine this with the original universal negative ('No J is B necessarily') to derive a contradiction: 'Some B is not B necessarily'. This proves the conversion without using the conversion of the particular affirmative.
This method uses a categorical syllogism: (Some B is J possibly, No J is B necessarily) → Some B is not B necessarily (contradiction).
4
Describe the hypothesis method (iftirad)
In the hypothesis method, we assume the converted universal negative is false, so 'Some B is J with possibility' is true. Then we hypothesize that this possible state is actual: 'Some B is J actually'. By substituting terms, we get that J is B and B is J, contradicting the original 'No J is B necessarily'.
This method relies on the principle that what is possible can be assumed actual without contradiction.
5
Conclude that circularity is avoided
Since both methods are independent and do not rely on the conversion of the particular affirmative, the proof of universal negative conversion is not circular. The objector's assumption of a single method is false.
The author also mentions an alternative response: the conversion of a possible proposition is self-evident, so the circle is broken anyway.

Conversion of Permanent Universal Negatives
Proof of conversion for permanent universal negatives and refutation of circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by contradiction for the conversion of a permanent universal negative proposition and evaluate the circularity objection.

Core Idea
A permanent 'No J is B' forces a permanent 'No B is J', and the proof avoids circularity via independent methods.

In this lesson, we examine the conversion rule for permanent universal negative propositions. The author proves that if something is never the case permanently, the converse also holds permanently. The proof uses contradiction and raises a familiar circularity concern, which is then resolved by citing two independent demonstrative methods. This is a key step in mastering immediate inferences in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original and converse
The original proposition is a permanent universal negative: 'No J is B permanently'. Its converse is 'No B is J permanently'. The author claims that if the original is true, the converse must also be true.
2
Assume the converse false
To prove by contradiction, assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory must be true: 'Some B is J with absolute temporal universality' (i.e., at all times).
Note: 'absolute temporal universality' (الإطلاق العام) means true at all times, just like 'permanent' but without modal necessity.
3
Convert the particular affirmative
The particular affirmative 'Some B is J (at all times)' converts partially to 'Some J is B (at all times)'. This is a known rule: a particular affirmative converts to a particular affirmative.
4
Derive contradiction
The converted proposition 'Some J is B (at all times)' contradicts the original 'No J is B permanently', because the original asserts that no J is B at any time. Hence the assumption that the converse is false leads to a contradiction, so the converse must be true.
5
Address circularity objection
An objector may say that this proof is circular because it uses the conversion of the particular affirmative, which itself needs proof. The author responds that the conversion of the particular can be demonstrated independently using the two methods of combination and hypothesis, as already known. Thus, no circularity occurs.
The two methods are standard techniques in logic to prove conversions without relying on other conversions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الدائمة فإنها تنعكس دائمة مثل نفسها؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) ب) دائمًا صدق قولنا لا شيء من «بج» دائمًا وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض (ب) ج) بالإطلاق العام وإنه ينعكس عكسا جزئيا وهو قولنا بعض ج ب بالإطلاق العام، وقد كان لا شيء من «ج ب» دائما، هذا خلف. ثم لقائل أن يقول فيه كما قيل من قبل في بيان انعكاس الضرورية وهو أنه من جملة ما يلزم منه الدور. لكنا نقول بمثلما قلناه من قبل أيضًا، فإنه يمكن أن تبين كل واحدة منهما بطريقي الانضمام والافتراض على ما عرف.
As for the permanent, it converts as permanent like itself. For if our statement 'No J is B permanently' is true, then our statement 'No B is J permanently' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some B is J with absolute temporal [universality]'. And it converts partially as our statement 'Some J is B with absolute temporal [universality]', but it was the case that 'No J is B permanently' — this is a contradiction. Then an objector may say about it as was said before in explaining the conversion of the necessary, that it is among what entails circularity. But we say the same as we said before: indeed, each of them can be demonstrated by the two methods of combination and hypothesis, as was known.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the conversion of permanent (دائمة) universal negative propositions. The proof is by contradiction: if the converted proposition were false, its contradictory (a particular affirmative with absolute temporal universality, i.e., true at all times) would be true, but that particular affirmative converts to a particular affirmative that contradicts the original permanent universal negative. Then the author notes that the same objection about circularity that was raised for necessary negatives is raised here. The response is the same as before: the two independent methods of combination and hypothesis can be used to prove each without circularity. Thus, the conversion of permanent negatives is established.

Key Terms
دائمة (da'ima): A permanent proposition: true at all times (e.g., 'No man is a stone permanently'). Not necessarily necessary, but always true. [Note: In some texts, 'دائمة' may be identified with absolute (مطلقة) propositions, but here it is a distinct mode.]
إطلاق عام (itlaq ‘amm): Absolute temporal universality: a proposition true at all times without qualification (e.g., 'Some man is an animal at all times'). [Note: This term is used in later modal logic; earlier texts may use 'mutlaqa' differently.]
انعكاس (‘aks): Conversion of a permanent universal negative to a permanent universal negative. [Note: Stable.]

Proving Conversion of the Possible Negative Universal
Author explains two methods for converting the possible negative universal proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof for the conversion of a universal negative possible proposition using either the method of combination or supposition.

Core Idea
Modal conversion requires proof beyond simple term swapping.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle issue in modal logic: how do we prove that a universal negative possible proposition ('No B is C with possibility') converts to its converse ('No C is B with possibility')? He presents two distinct methods—combination and supposition—that use different strategies to derive a contradiction if the converse were false. These methods reveal how medieval Islamic logicians extended Aristotelian conversion to modal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion claim
The claim is that a universal negative possible proposition (e.g., 'No human is a horse with possibility') converts to another universal negative possible proposition ('No horse is a human with possibility'). The author provides two proofs to demonstrate this validity.
The original and converse share the same modality (possibility).
2
Follow the method of combination
Assume the contradictory of the desired converse (an affirmative particular possible: 'Some human is a horse with possibility') is true. Combine this with the original proposition ('No horse is a human with possibility') and deduce a contradiction (e.g., 'Some human is not human'), showing the assumption is false, hence the converse holds.
The original proposition is taken as a necessary premise in this proof.
3
Follow the method of supposition
Assume the converse is false, so its contradictory ('Some human is a horse with possibility') is true. Since it is possible, we can suppose it is actually true. Then, from the actual state, derive a contradiction with the original ('No horse is a human with necessity'?), forcing the converse to be true.
This method relies on the principle that what is possible can be assumed actual without contradiction.
4
Compare the two methods
Both methods achieve the same result but through different logical moves: combination uses the contradictory directly, while supposition actualizes the possible. Each highlights a different aspect of modal reasoning.
5
Evaluate the self-evidence objection
Some logicians argue that the conversion is self-evident and needs no proof. The author acknowledges this but notes that further discussion is needed, implying that rigorous proof remains valuable in systematic logic.
This objection raises questions about the role of proof in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألا ترى أنه يمكن بيان كل واحدة منهما بطريق الانضمام تارةً، وبطريق الافتراض أخرى. [1] أما طريق الانضمام فذلك مثل ما يقال إنه إذا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام يضم هذا إلى الأصل فيقال بعض ب ج بالإمكان العام، ولا شيء من ج ب بالضرورة؛ فبعض ب ليس (ب) بالضرورة، هذا خلف. [ب] وأما طريق الافتراض فإن يقال إذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من ج ب بالضرورة صدق قولنا لا شيء من ب ج ) بالضرورة وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض اب ج» بالإمكان العام فنفرضه أن يكون بالفعل فيكون بعض ب ج بالفعل، فذلك البعض بعينه «ب» و «ج» فذلك الجيم «ب» وذلك الباء (ج) لا محالة. وقد قلنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب»، هذا خلف. فلا يستراب في أن فرض الممكن موجودًا غير ممتنع وإلا لا يكون ممكناً؛ والكلام في كونه ممكنا. وعن البعض منهم في الجواب عن الشبهة المذكورة أن انعكاس الممكن ممكناً بين بنفسه. فإنه إذا أمكن أن يكون هذا الشيء ذلك أمكن أن يكون ذلك الشيء هذا. ولما كان هذا بينا بنفسه جاز تعريف غيره به من غير أن يفتقر تعريفه إلى تعريف ذلك الغير غير أنه لا يصح إلا وأن يكون بينا بنفسه؛ وفيه من الكلام
Do you not see that each of them can be demonstrated either by the method of combination or by the method of supposition. [1] As for the method of combination, that is like saying: if its contradictory is true, which is our statement 'Some B is C with general possibility', this is combined with the original to say 'Some B is C with general possibility', and 'No C is B with necessity'; so 'Some B is not B with necessity' – this is a contradiction. [B] As for the method of supposition, that is: if our statement 'No C is B with necessity' is true, our statement 'No B is C with necessity' is true, otherwise its contradictory 'Some B is C with general possibility' would be true, and we suppose it to be actual, so 'Some B is C actually'. That same (B) and (C): that C is B and that B is C necessarily. And we said 'No C is B with necessity' – this is a contradiction. So there is no doubt that supposing the possible to exist is not impossible, otherwise it would not be possible; and the discourse is about its being possible. And from some of them in answer to the mentioned doubt: the conversion of the possible is self-evident. For if it is possible that this thing is that, then it is possible that that thing is this. And since this is self-evident, it is permissible to define other things by it without its definition requiring the definition of that other – except that it is only valid if it is self-evident; and there is discussion in that.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of the negative universal possible proposition. The author presents two proof methods: combination (الانضمام) and supposition (الافتراض). In the combination method, one takes the contradictory of the desired converse and combines it with the original premise to derive a contradiction. In the supposition method, one hypothetically actualizes the possible to force a contradiction. After presenting these proofs, the author mentions a rebuttal from some scholars who claim that the conversion of the possible is self-evident and does not require proof. The author acknowledges this view but notes there is further discussion.

Conversion of the Perpetual Negative Universal
Author proves the perpetual negative universal converts to a similar perpetual proposition and defends against the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reproduce the proof that the perpetual negative universal proposition converts to a perpetual negative universal, using contradiction and partial conversion, and evaluate the circularity objection.

Core Idea
The perpetual negative universal converts to itself without change of modality.

In this passage, the author demonstrates that a perpetual negative universal proposition (e.g., 'No C is B perpetually') validly converts to a similar proposition ('No B is C perpetually'). The proof uses a contradiction argument: assuming the converse is false leads to a contradictory absolute proposition, which via partial conversion contradicts the original. The author also anticipates and refutes an objection that this proof involves circularity, by citing alternative proof methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion claim
Find the statement where the author asserts that the perpetual negative universal converts to a perpetual like itself. Mark the original and converse forms.
The claim is at the beginning: 'وأما في الدائمة فإنها تنعكس دائمة مثل نفسها'
2
Trace the contradiction proof
Follow the logical steps: assume the converse is false, which makes its contradictory true (an absolute particular affirmative). Then note that this absolute proposition partially converts to an absolute particular affirmative that contradicts the original perpetual universal.
The contradictory of 'لا شيء من (ب) ج) دائمًا' is 'بعض (ب) ج) بالإطلاق العام'.
3
Examine the partial conversion step
The absolute particular affirmative 'Some B is C with absolute generality' partially converts to 'Some C is B with absolute generality'. This directly contradicts the original 'No C is B perpetually'.
Partial conversion of an absolute particular affirmative yields an absolute particular affirmative.
4
Analyze the circularity objection
Note that an objector claims the proof involves circularity (دور) because it relies on conversion of absolute propositions, which might itself depend on the perpetual conversion. The author responds that both can be proven independently via combination and supposition methods.
The author's response implies that the proof is not circular because absolute conversion is independently established.

Conversion of the Universal Conditional Negative
Author proves the universal conditional negative converts to a similar conditional proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a universal conditional negative proposition and verify the conversion using the method of contradiction.

Core Idea
A universal conditional negative converts to itself with swapped terms and a changed condition.

In this lesson, we examine the conversion rule for the universal conditional negative proposition. This proposition asserts that, necessarily, no member of the subject class is a member of the predicate class as long as the subject exists. The author demonstrates that it converts to a similar proposition with the subject and predicate swapped, and the condition adjusted to the new subject. Understanding this conversion is essential for handling modal propositions in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original universal conditional negative has the form 'Necessarily, no C is B as long as C exists'. The condition 'as long as C' restricts the universality to the duration of the subject's existence.
The terms are C and B, and the condition is tied to C.
2
State the converse proposition
The author claims it converts to 'Necessarily, no B is C as long as B exists'. The terms are swapped, and the condition now applies to B.
The condition changes from 'as long as C' to 'as long as B'.
3
Apply proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory must be true: 'Some B is C when it is B with general possibility'. But this contradicts the original proposition, which says 'No C is B necessarily'. Therefore the converse must be true.
The partial converse 'Some C is B with general possibility' also contradicts the original if the original were universal.
4
Verify the condition consistency
The condition 'as long as' is tied to the existence of the subject in each proposition. In the converse, the condition applies to B, consistent with the swapped subject.
This ensures the proposition remains universal conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشروطة العامة فإنها تنعكس كنفسها كذلك؛ فإنه إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج) ب ما دام «ج»، فقد صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء « من ب ج ما دام «ب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ب ج ) حين هو «ب» بالإمكان العام. وإنه ينعكس عكسا جزئيا وهو قولنا بعض «ج ب» بالإمكان العام، وقد كان لا شيء من ج ب بالضرورة، هذا خلف. ويمكن أن يتبين بطريقي الانضمام والافتراض أيضًا كما مرَّ من قبل؛ وهذا ظاهر.
As for the universal conditional [proposition], it converts like itself as well. For if our statement 'Necessarily no C is B as long as C' is true, then our statement 'Necessarily no B is C as long as B' is true; otherwise its contradictory, which is our statement 'Some B is C when it is B with general possibility', would be true. And it partially converts to our statement 'Some C is B with general possibility', but we had 'No C is B necessarily' – this is a contradiction. And it can be demonstrated by the two methods of combination and supposition as previously; and this is evident.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage covers the conversion of the universal conditional negative proposition (المشروطة العامة). The proposition is of the form 'Necessarily, no C is B as long as C exists'. The author asserts that it converts to 'Necessarily, no B is C as long as B exists'. The proof is again by contradiction: assuming the converse false leads to a contradictory partial converse that contradicts the original. The author notes that the two methods (combination and supposition) can be applied here as well, and considers the result evident.

Conversion of the General Customary Negative
Proof that the general customary negative converts to a similar proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conversion rule for the general customary negative proposition and produce a valid proof for it.

Core Idea
The general customary negative converts to itself.

In this lesson, we explore how the general customary negative ('No C is B as long as C') converts to a proposition of the same form. The author provides a proof by contradiction, similar to earlier cases. This is a key immediate inference in post-Avicennan logic, distinct from the universal conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The source proposition is the general customary negative: 'No C is B as long as C' (لا شيء من ج ب ما دام ج). Note its subject (ج), predicate (ب), and the condition 'as long as C'.
2
State the converse
The author claims it converts to 'No B is C as long as B'. Write both propositions and observe that the subject-predicate roles are swapped while the condition changes to 'as long as B'.
3
Apply the proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory, 'Some B is C with absolute generality' (بعض ب ج حين هو ب بالإطلاق العام), must be true. This proposition partially converts to 'Some C is B with absolute generality', which contradicts the original universal negative. Hence the converse must be true.
The proof mirrors that for the universal conditional.
4
Recognize standard proof methods
The author notes that two methods (combination and supposition) can also be used, but since they are identical to previous examples, they are omitted. Understand that these are general tools for verifying conversions.

Conversion of the Particular Conditional Negative
Author argues that the particular conditional negative does not convert to itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the particular conditional negative does not convert to itself and identify the correct conversion according to different scholars.

Core Idea
A particular conditional negative cannot convert uniformly due to asymmetry in perpetuity.

In this lesson, we explore an important subtlety in proposition conversion, specifically for the particular conditional negative (المشروطة الخاصة). The source passage demonstrates that this proposition type does not convert to itself, and different early logicians proposed different solutions. Understanding this asymmetrical behavior is crucial for evaluating the validity of arguments in Islamic sciences that rely on conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition type
The particular conditional negative (المشروطة الخاصة) has the form: 'Necessarily no C is B, not perpetually but as long as C exists.' Note the 'not perpetually' qualifier.
This is a conditional proposition with a temporal qualification.
2
State the attempted conversion
The conversion attempts to swap the terms, producing: 'Necessarily no B is C, not perpetually but as long as B exists.' The question is whether this follows logically.
3
Test with a counterexample
Consider that some B might be B (i.e., exist) perpetually, while others are not. The conversion would then be 'not perpetually' for some instances and 'perpetually' for others, which fails to match the universal 'not perpetually' of the original.
This shows the asymmetry.
4
Analyze scholarly positions
Early scholars differed: some held the conversion is that of the general universal conditional (عامتها), because the particular shares a common element. Others argued the conversion must account for the possibility of partial perpetuity.
The author leans toward the impossibility of uniform conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشروطة الخاصة فإنها لا تنعكس كنفسها البتة، فإنه إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب لا دائما بل ما دام «ج» لا يلزم أن يصدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من (ب) (ج) لا دائما بل ما دام «ب». فإن من الممكن أن يكون بعض «ب» هو «ب» دائمًا بل يلزم منه أن يصدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ب ج » حين هو «ب» دائمًا في البعض لا دائما في البعض، وهذا هو العكس عند البعض من المتقدمين. وعند بعضهم عكسها هو عكس عامتها لما أنه هو المشترك بين أن يكون دائما في البعض وبين أن لا يكون دائما. ومنهم من أتى ذلك وهو أن عكسها عكس عامتها لما أن العامة منعكسة كنفسها كما، ونفسها محتملة للضرورة والدوام؛ ومن المحال أن يكون اللا دائم دائما.
As for the particular conditional [proposition], it does not convert to itself at all. For if our statement 'Necessarily no C is B, not perpetually but as long as C' is true, it does not imply that our statement 'Necessarily no B is C, not perpetually but as long as B' is true. For it is possible that some B is B perpetually, rather it implies that our statement 'Necessarily no B is C when it is B' is true perpetually for some [Bs] and not perpetually for others. And this is the conversion according to some of the earlier scholars. And according to some of them, its conversion is the conversion of its general [type] because it is common between being perpetual for some and not perpetual. And among them is one who brought that: its conversion is the conversion of its general because the general converts like itself, and itself is susceptible to necessity and perpetuity; and it is impossible for the non-perpetual to be perpetual.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage treats the particular conditional negative proposition (المشروطة الخاصة), which asserts 'Necessarily no C is B, not perpetually but only as long as C exists'. The author argues that it does not convert to a proposition of the same type. The reason is that while the original says the negation holds only conditionally, the converse might be perpetual for some instances of B and not for others, so it cannot be uniformly 'not perpetual'. There is disagreement among earlier scholars: some consider the conversion to be the same as that of the universal conditional (عامتها), because the particular conditional shares a common element with the universal. The author concludes by noting that the universal conditional converts to itself, but the particular cannot because it would require the non-perpetual to become perpetual, which is impossible.

Key Terms
مشروطة خاصة
لا دائما

Key Takeaway
A particular conditional negative cannot be converted to itself because the 'not perpetually' condition does not hold uniformly in the converse.

Conversion of General Conditioned and General Conventional Negatives
Author explains conversion rules for general conditioned and general conventional negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the conversion rule for general conditioned and general conventional universal negative propositions.

Core Idea
General universal negatives convert like themselves: converse remains universal, negative, and temporally conditioned.

In this passage, the author explains how two specific types of universal negative propositions—general conditioned (mashrutah 'ammah) and general conventional (urfiyyah 'ammah)—convert (aks) into propositions of the same type. Understanding this rule is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in modal logic, especially in Islamic theological and legal arguments that involve temporal conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize the two types: general conditioned (with necessity) and general conventional (without necessity). Both are universal negatives with a temporal clause: 'as long as the subject exists'.
The general conditioned adds 'necessarily' (bi’l-darurah), while the general conventional uses absolute generality (bi’l-itlaq al-‘amm).
2
State the conversion rule
Each of these propositions converts 'like itself'—the converse is also a universal negative with the same temporal condition. For example, 'Necessarily, no J is B as long as J' converts to 'Necessarily, no B is J as long as B'.
3
Follow the reductio proof (khalaf)
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory (a particular affirmative) would be true. Show that this contradicts the original proposition. For the general conditioned, the contradictory is 'Some B is J when it is B with general possibility' (bi’l-imkan al-‘amm). This would imply 'some J is B with possibility', which contradicts the original necessary universal negative.
The same reasoning applies to the general conventional, but the contradictory uses 'with absolute generality' instead of possibility.
4
Note alternative proof methods
The author mentions that the conversion can also be proven by composition (indimam) and supposition (iftirad), but these are similar to earlier discussions and not expanded here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] وأما المشروطة العامة فإنها تنعكس كنفسها كذلك؛ فإنه إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب ما دام «ج»، فقد صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ب ج ما دام «ب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ب ج حين هو «ب» بالإمكان العام. وإنه ينعكس عكسا جزئيا وهو قولنا بعض ج ب بالإمكان العام، وقد كان لا شيء من ج ب بالضرورة، هذا خلف. ويمكن أن يتبين بطريقي الانضمام والافتراض أيضًا كما مرَّ من قبل؛ وهذا ظاهر. [٤] وأما العرفية العامة فإنها تنعكس كنفسها أيضًا، فإنه إذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من ج ب ما دام (ج) صدق قولنا لا شيء من ب ج ما دام «ب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ب ج حين هو «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وإنه ينعكس عكسا جزئياً؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال كما مر. ويمكن أن يتبين أيضًا بطريقي الانضمام والافتراض؛ وإنهما في هذه الصورة وفي الصورة التي تقدم ذكرها على السواء فلا حاجة إلى التعرُّض ببيانهما صريحًا.
[3] As for the general conditioned (al-mashrutatu 'l-‘ammah), it converts like itself likewise: for if our statement 'Necessarily, no J is B as long as J' is true, then our statement 'Necessarily, no B is J as long as B' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some B is J when it is B with general possibility (bi’l-imkani ‘l-‘amm)'. And it converts partially, which is our statement 'Some J is B with general possibility', whereas it was 'No J is B necessarily', which is an absurdity (khalaf). It can also be demonstrated by the methods of composition (indimam) and supposition (iftirad), as previously mentioned; and this is clear. [4] As for the general conventional (al-‘urfiyatu ‘l-‘ammah), it also converts like itself: for if our statement 'No J is B as long as J' is true, our statement 'No B is J as long as B' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some B is J when it is B with absolute generality (bi’l-itlaqi ‘l-‘amm)'. And it converts partially; and that leads to an impossibility (muhal) as previously. It can also be demonstrated by the methods of composition and supposition; and these two methods are the same in this form and the form mentioned earlier, so there is no need to explicitly discuss them.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage details the conversion (aks) of two types of general universal negatives: the general conditioned (mashrutah ‘ammah) and the general conventional (urfiyyah ‘ammah). The author first states that they convert like themselves—i.e., a universal negative remains universal negative after conversion. He then provides a proof by reductio (khalaf). For the general conditioned: if 'Necessarily, no J is B as long as J' is true, then 'Necessarily, no B is J as long as B' must also be true. The contradictory of the latter would be a particular affirmative with general possibility, which would contradict the original. Similarly for the general conventional: if 'No J is B as long as J' is true, then 'No B is J as long as B' is true; its contradictory would be a particular affirmative with absolute generality, leading to absurdity. The author notes that these can also be proven by the methods of indimam (composition) and iftirad (supposition), which he does not expand upon here as they are similar to earlier discussions. The key point is that both types of general negatives preserve their quantity and modality when converted.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
العرفية العامة
عكس
بالضرورة
بالإمكان العام

D) Some intoxicant is permissible with necessity.

Key Takeaway
General universal negatives with temporal conditions convert to themselves: the converse retains the same quantity, quality, modality, and temporal condition, as proven by reductio.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Particular Conditioned and Conventional Negatives Do Not Convert Like Themselves
Author refutes that particular conditioned and particular conventional negatives convert like themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct converse of a particular conditioned or conventional negative proposition and distinguish it from the mistaken conversion.

Core Idea
A particular temporal condition in a universal negative blocks simple conversion.

In this lesson, we examine why particular conditioned (mashrutah khassah) and particular conventional (urfiyyah khassah) propositions cannot be converted like themselves. The author shows that the temporal restriction 'as long as the subject exists' prevents the symmetrical conversion that works for general types. We'll walk through the logical reasoning and see how early logicians debated the proper converse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proposition forms
The 'particular conditioned' is a universal negative that is true necessarily but only as long as the subject exists, e.g., 'Necessarily, no J is B not always but only as long as J'. The 'particular conventional' is similar but without necessity. Note the temporal restriction 'only as long as J'.
These are not particular in quantity; they are universal in scope but restricted in time.
2
Test conversion hypothetically
Suppose we try to convert the proposition by swapping subject and predicate while keeping the same temporal form: from 'Necessarily, no J is B as long as J' we infer 'Necessarily, no B is J as long as B'. The author shows this does not follow because some B might always be B, meaning the condition 'as long as B' could hold eternally for some instances, violating the original's non-perpetual character.
The reason: the original proposition does not guarantee that B is not always B.
3
Identify what does follow
From the original, we can infer a universal negative that is true always in some cases but not always in others. For example, 'Necessarily, no B is J when it is B' is true always in some cases (where B is not perpetual) and not always in others (where B is perpetual). This split modality is the correct converse according to the author.
Some early logicians accepted this split converse; others argued for the converse of the general type.
4
Compare with the general type
Some scholars claimed the converse is the same as the converse of the general type (e.g., 'Necessarily, no B is J as long as B'), because that type is common between perpetual and non-perpetual. The author rejects this because a non-perpetual condition cannot be applied universally without contradiction.
The general type converts like itself only when the condition is perpetual or indefinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٥] وأما المشروطة الخاصة فإنها لا تنعكس كنفسها البتة، فإنه إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب لا دائما بل ما دام «ج» لا يلزم أن يصدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من (ب) (ج) لا دائما بل ما دام «ب». فإن من الممكن أن يكون بعض «ب» هو «ب» دائمًا بل يلزم منه أن يصدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ب ج حين هو «ب» دائمًا في البعض لا دائما في البعض، وهذا هو العكس عند بعض المتقدمين. وعند بعضهم عكسها هو عكس عامتها لما أنه هو المشترك بين أن يكون دائما في البعض وبين أن لا يكون دائما. ومنهم من أتى ذلك وهو أن عكسها عكس عامتها لما أن العامة منعكسة كنفسها كما، ونفسها محتملة للضرورة والدوام؛ ومن المحال أن يكون اللا دائم دائما . [٦] وأما العرفية الخاصة فقد قيل إنها تنعكس كنفسها»؛ وذلك خطأ. فإنه لا يلزم من صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) (ب) لا دائما بل ما دام «ج» صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ب) ج) لا دائمًا بل ما دام «ب» وقد كان من الممكن أن يكون بعض «ب» هو «ب» دائمًا بل يلزم منه أن يصدق لا شيء من «بج» حين هو «ب» دائما في البعض، لادائما في البعض. وأما أن هذا هو العكس أو عكسها هو عكس العامة؛ فالكلام فيه نحو الكلام في المشروطة الخاصة.
[5] As for the particular conditioned (al-mashrutatu 'l-khassah), it does not convert like itself at all. For if our statement 'Necessarily, no J is B not always but only as long as J' is true, it does not follow that our statement 'Necessarily, no B is J not always but only as long as B' is true. For it is possible that some B is B always. Rather, it follows from it that our statement 'Necessarily, no B is J when it is B' is true always in some cases but not always in some cases. And this is the conversion according to some of the earlier scholars. According to others, its conversion is the conversion of its general type, because that type is common between being always in some cases and not always. And some have come with this, that its conversion is the conversion of its general type because the general type converts like itself, and itself admits necessity and perpetuity; and it is impossible for what is not perpetual to be perpetual. [6] As for the particular conventional (al-‘urfiyatu 'l-khassah), it has been said that it converts like itself; but this is a mistake. For it does not follow from the truth of our statement 'No J is B not always but only as long as J' that our statement 'No B is J not always but only as long as B' is true, because it is possible that some B is B always. Rather, it follows from it that 'No B is J when it is B' is true always in some cases, not always in some cases. As for whether this is the conversion or its conversion is the conversion of the general type, the discussion is like the discussion concerning the particular conditioned.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage addresses two types of particular negatives: the particular conditioned (mashrutah khassah) and the particular conventional (urfiyyah khassah). The author first states unequivocally that the particular conditioned does not convert like itself. The proof: from the original 'Necessarily, no J is B not always but only as long as J', we cannot infer a converse with the same temporal condition because some B might always be B. Instead, the converse that follows is a universal negative with a split modality: always in some cases, not always in others. The author notes that different early logicians had different views on what the proper converse is—some considered this split converse as the aks, while others argued that the aks is the same as the aks of the general type (i.e., a universal negative with the condition 'as long as B'), since that is common between perpetual and non-perpetual cases. The argument for the latter involves the impossibility of a non-perpetual being perpetual. For the particular conventional, a claim that it converts like itself is declared a mistake. The reasoning is analogous: from 'No J is B not always but only as long as J' we cannot infer 'No B is J not always but only as long as B' because some B might always be B. The proper converse is again a universal negative true always for some instances and not for others. The author concludes that the discussion about whether this split converse or the general-type conversion is correct is the same as for the particular conditioned.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة
العرفية الخاصة
عكسها هو عكس عامتها
الا دائما
ما دام

Checkpoint
If the proposition 'Necessarily, no adulterer (zani) is married (muhsan) not always but only as long as he is an adulterer' is true, which of the following is a valid converse according to the author?
A) Necessarily, no married person is an adulterer not always but only as long as they are married.
B) Necessarily, no married person is an adulterer always in some cases, not always in others.
C) No married person is an adulterer as long as they are married.
D) Some married person is an adulterer with possibility.

Key Takeaway
In converting particular conditioned or conventional negatives, the temporal restriction cannot be simply transferred; the correct converse has a split modality reflecting the possibility of the predicate term being perpetual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
